Aims and Scope
The series “Springer Theses” brings together a selection of the very best Ph.D.
theses from around the world and across the physical sciences. Nominated and
endorsed by two recognized specialists, each published volume has been selected
for its scientiﬁc excellence and the high impact of its contents for the pertinent ﬁeld
of research. For greater accessibility to non-specialists, the published versions
include an extended introduction, as well as a foreword by the student’s supervisor
explaining the special relevance of the work for the ﬁeld. As a whole, the series will
provide a valuable resource both for newcomers to the research ﬁelds described,
and for other scientists seeking detailed background information on special
questions. Finally, it provides an accredited documentation of the valuable
contributions made by today’s younger generation of scientists.
Theses are accepted into the series by invited nomination only
and must fulﬁll all of the following criteria
• They must be written in good English.
• The topic should fall within the conﬁnes of Chemistry, Physics, Earth Sciences,
Engineering and related interdisciplinary ﬁelds such as Materials, Nanoscience,
Chemical Engineering, Complex Systems and Biophysics.
• The work reported in the thesis must represent a signiﬁcant scientiﬁc advance.
• If the thesis includes previously published material, permission to reproduce this
must be gained from the respective copyright holder.
• They must have been examined and passed during the 12 months prior to
nomination.
• Each thesis should include a foreword by the supervisor outlining the signiﬁ-
cance of its content.
• The theses should have a clearly deﬁned structure including an introduction
accessible to scientists not expert in that particular ﬁeld.
More information about this series at http://www.springer.com/series/8790

Supervisor’s Foreword
The discovery of superconductivity in Fe-based materials in 2008 boosted the
researches in high-Tc superconductivity. Although the high-Tc superconductivity in
copper oxides (cuprates) was discovered in 1986, no consensus has been achieved
on the microscopic mechanism in spite of considerable efforts. The discovery of
Fe-based superconductors (FeSC) provided a new route to high-Tc superconduc-
tivity. Since superconductivity in both cuprates and FeSC arises from doped
antiferromagnets, unveiling the interplay between superconductivity and magnetic
order, spin dynamics, and related exotic phases are essential for understanding the
microscopic origin of high-Tc superconductivity.
The aim of this thesis is to reveal this interplay between superconductivity and
other orders and their dynamics in a speciﬁc electron-doped iron-pnictide system
BaFe2−xNixAs2. Using high-resolution X-ray diffraction, neutron diffraction, and
inelastic neutron scattering (INS), Xingye Lu studied the phase diagram and the
spin excitations of BaFe2−xNixAs2. In his ﬁrst work (Chap. 4), he used
high-resolution X-ray and neutron diffraction to establish a detailed structural,
magnetic, and electronic phase diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2. The magnetic and
orthorhombic structural order parameters reveal a strong magnetoelastic coupling,
which was expected to lead to a quantum bicritical point near optimal doping.
However, the emergence of an incommensurate antiferromagnetic order close to
optimal superconductivity strongly modiﬁes the electronic properties and pre-
empted the purported quantum critical point. This work clariﬁes the quantum
criticality and its connection to superconductivity in electron-doped BaFe2As2
systems. To further investigate the nature of the short-range incommensurate
magnetic order, he exploited neutron resonance spin-echo technique, cold, thermal,
and polarized neutron triple-axis spectrometers to study the slow dynamics of the
incommensurate magnetic order, and determined its cluster spin glass nature
(Chap. 5). These two works have settled the doping evolution of the magnetic
ground states and their effects on the electronic properties of this system.
After determining the phase diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2, Xingye used INS to
investigate the newly discovered electronic nematic order located between Ts and
TN (Ts [ TN) in the phase diagram (Chap. 6). This study is difﬁcult because it will
v

need to be performed on large detwinned crystals (*0.2 g). After putting great
efforts, he developed a method to prepare sample and designed a detwinning device
that can detwin large single crystal. His INS studies on BaFe2−xNixAs2 ﬁnd that the
low-energy spin excitations evolve from C4 symmetry (with equal intensity for the
excitations at (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1)) to C2 symmetry (with intensity difference
between the excitations at (1,0,1) and (0, 1, 1)). The onset temperature of this
spin-excitation anisotropy (T) is well above Ts and coincident with that for the
resistivity anisotropy of uniaxial-stress detwinned BaFe2−xNixAs2. They also show
similar doping dependence and therefore indicate that they are intimately correlated
or share the same origin. This is the ﬁrst study of this type and inspired many
follow-up works.
In the third part of this thesis, he unveiled the direct correlation between
low-energy spin excitations (including spin resonance) and superconductivity, and
the persistence of the diffusive high-energy spin excitations (paramagnon)
throughout the phase diagram, using the state-of-art time-of-ﬂight neutron scatter-
ing spectrometers in ISIS (Chap. 7). This work provided solid evidence that the
low-energy spin excitations are correlated with the formation of cooper pairs. The
systematic work was based on large amount of single crystals (*100 g of single
crystals in total for three dopings) and careful INS experiments. This impressive
sample growth work was done within several months by Xingye and his colleagues.
Besides the important discoveries discussed above, this thesis contains a brief
introduction to FeSC and a relative detailed introduction to the principles and
techniques associated with neutron scattering. Both parts are easy to follow by
non-experts. Overall, the work of Xingye has broadened considerably our under-
standing of the interplay between magnetism and superconductivity in FeSC.
Through his dedication, patience, and hard work, he was able to accomplish a
tremendous amount of work within a relatively short period. I believe his thesis sets
a new standard of excellence in condensed matter physics and will be appreciated
for many years to come.
Houston, USA
April 2017
Prof. Pengcheng Dai

Chapter 1
Motivation and Overview
1.1
Superconductivity
Superconductivity refers to a phenomenon of exact zero resistivity of certain material
(termed “superconductor”) when it is cooled below a critical temperature (supercon-
ducting transition temperature Tc). It was discovered in mercury by H. Kamerlingh
Onnes in 1911 (Fig.1.1a) [1], soon after he liquiﬁed Helium and obtained very low
temperature (<4.2 K). The other basic phenomenon for superconductivity is the per-
fect diamagnetism discovered by Meissner and Ochsenfeld in 1933 (Meissner effect
[2]) (Fig.1.1b), which shows that magnetic ﬁeld (B) in a superconductor is expelled
while the superconductor is cooled below Tc. Zero resistance and Meissner effect are
basic criteria of a superconducting state and serve as the starting point for developing
theories of superconductivity [3].
Phenomenological theories concerning the electrodynamics of superconductivity
were developed by London in 1935 [4] and improved by Pippard in 1953 [3]. In
1950, Ginzburg-Landau (GL) theory of superconductivity was proposed based on the
Landau theory of second-order phase transition by treating superconducting wave
function as an order parameter of superconducting state. London theory and GL
theory introduced several important concepts such as critical ﬁeld (Hc), penetration
depth (λ) and coherence length (ξ) of superconductivity and have achieved great
success in the studies of superconductivity [5, 6].
In 1946, F. London proposed that superconductivity is a macroscopic quantum
phenomenon, in which he suggested that all the superconducting electrons are in one
quantum state of a wave function (ψ) [6]. This idea was demonstrated by the develop-
ment of microscopic theory of superconductivity. In 1957, J. Bardeen, L. N. Copper
and J. R. Schrieffer established the quantum theory for superconductivity (BCS the-
ory) in a mean-ﬁeld theoretical regime [7], where superconductivity is suggested to
be driven by electron-phonon coupling. Because of the attractive interactions caused
by electron-phonon coupling, electrons close to Fermi surface tend to form binding
electron pairs (Copper pairs). The Copper pairs condense into a superﬂuid state and
lead to the perfect conductivity [7].

X. Lu, Phase Diagram and Magnetic Excitations of BaFe2−xNixAs2: A Neutron
Scattering Study, Springer Theses, DOI 10.1007/978-981-10-4998-9_1

Motivation and Overview

Fig. 1.1 a The discovery of superconductivity by H. K. Onnes in 1911. The resistance of Hg
disappears suddenly below 4.2 K, indicating a superconducting state [1]. b Perfect diamagnetism
discovered by Meissner and Ochsenfeld [2]
The condensation redistributes the electron density of states close to Fermi sur-
face and open a superconducting energy gap, which protects the superconducting
state against impurity scattering and other weak perturbations. Within the coherent
length, superconductingstatehas uniformenergygapandphasefactor, indicatingthat
superconductivity is a macroscopic quantum phenomenon. Basic macroscopic phe-
nomena of superconductivity include zero resistivity, Meissner effect, abrupt change
of electron speciﬁc heat, magnetic ﬂux quantization in a superconducting ring and
Josephson effect [5, 6, 8].
Besides revealing the weak-coupling nature and calculating various basic proper-
ties of the conventional superconductivity in many metals and simple alloys, the BCS
theory also unveiled the s-wave symmetry of the superconducting state. Another
major contribution made by BCS theory is some basic concepts and methods in
describing superconductivity. For example, the description of the superconducting
energy gap and pairing symmetry introduced by BCS theory can be applied to non-
BCS superconductors.
In BSC theory, the pairing mechanism is electron-phonon coupling. Since the
strength of the electron-phonon coupling depends on Debye frequency and controls
the superconducting transition temperature Tc, the Tc of conventional superconduc-
tivity has its upper limit. McMilan predicted that the highest Tc driven by electron-
phonon coupling is about 40 K [9]. Thus far, the highest Tc of BCS superconductors
is 39 K for MgB2 [10]. Before 1986, the highest Tc achieved is 23K of Nb3Ge [11].
In 1986, Bednoz and Müller discovered novel superconductivity with Tc above
30K in copper oxides [12], which triggered numerous exploration of high tem-

1.1 Superconductivity
3
perature superconductors. The highest Tc record was improved frequently within a
short time. Several classes of copper-oxide high temperature superconductors with
Tc above 77K were discovered [3, 13–17]. Compared with conventional BCS super-
conductors, cuprate high temperature superconductors exhibit more exotic features
and cannot be described by BCS theory. The superconductors described by BCS
theory is termed “conventional” superconductors, whereas all other superconduc-
tors are called “unconventional” superconductors. Three classes of unconventional
superconductors have been discovered and received extensive studies: copper-oxide
superconductors, iron-based superconductors and heavy-Fermion superconductors
[18]. Iron-based superconductivity was discovered in LaOFeP (Tc = 8 K) by Hosono
in Tokyo Institute of Technology [19]. In 2008, the same group discovered super-
conductivity with Tc = 26K in LaFeAsO1−xFx [20], which is the beginning of the
intensive studies of iron-based superconductors. Later, Hosono [21], Chen [22] and
Wang [23] improved Tc to above 40K via substituting La by other rear-earth ele-
ments or applying hydrostatic high pressure. Using high-pressure synthesis method,
Ren et al. obtained Tc = 55K in Sm[O1−xFx]FeAs, which is a record of Tc in bulk
iron-based superconductors [24, 25].
Since the discovery of superconductivity, the research associated with supercon-
ductivity focus on four aspects: (1) exploring novel superconductors with higher Tc;
(2) understanding the properties of superconductivity using both phenomenological
and microscopic theory (such as GL and BCS); (3) establishing the microscopic
theory for unconventional superconductivity; (4) applications of superconductors.
Though three decades have been past, no consensus has been achieved on the micro-
scopic origin of the unconventional (or high temperature) superconductivity.
1.2
Scope of This Thesis
In spite of great progress in the research of cuprate and iron-based high-Tc super-
conductivity [17, 18, 26–32], the underlying microscopic pairing mechanism for
superconductivity remains elusive and is still an important frontier of modern con-
densed matter physics. Because of the failure of BCS theory in explaining high-Tc
superconductivity, new microscopic mechanisms are required [33, 34]. Since the
optimal superconductivity appears at the border of the antiferromagnetic instability,
spin ﬂuctuations persisting in optimal and overdoped regime of high-Tc supercon-
ductors could play an important role in driving superconductivity [32, 35–41].
In neutron scattering studies of high-Tc superconductivity, a ubiquitous spin-1
resonance mode in low energy spin excitations [42] was discovered in the supercon-
ducting state of numerous unconventional superconductors and shows a universal
ratio between resonance energy and Tc (and energy gap) [34, 43–49]. The temper-
ature dependence of the spin resonance is coincident with that for superconducting
gap. With increasing doping, the intensity of the spin resonance follows the doping
evolution of superconductivity [50, 51]. On the other hand, high energy spin exci-
tations persist throughout the whole phase diagram in various systems and could

4
1
Motivation and Overview
be a context for the emergence of superconductivity. Therefore, spin ﬂuctuation is
deemed as a leading candidate for mediating cooper pairs. Understanding the origin
of the magnetism and its relationship with superconductivity is crucial for revealing
the microscopic origin of high-Tc superconductivity. In iron pnictides, the micro-
scopic origin of the magnetism itself is still under debate, as well as its correlation
with superconductivity. In order to clarify this issue and improve our understanding
on these questions, we perform detailed neutron scattering studies on electron-doped
BaFe2−xNixAs2 for its magnetic order and spin dynamics, from which we can reveal
the origin of the magnetism and the correlation of the spin excitations with high-Tc
superconductivity. The research of this thesis consist of three parts listed as follows.
(1) (Chapters4 and 5) Neutron diffraction and high resolution x-ray diffraction
have been applied to study the doping range near optimal superconductivity to estab-
lish a detailed electronic phase diagram and to reveal the effects of short-range
incommensurate magnetic order on optimal doping region. The evolution of the
antiferromagnetic order and the orthorhombic lattice distortion with temperature
indicates a strong magnetoelastic coupling in this system, with which theoretically
Ts and TN tend to converge near optimal doping and form a quantum bicritical
point. However, the zero-temperature transition is preempted by the formation of the
incommensurate order in the region 0.088 < x < 0.104, resulting in a ﬁnite value
of TN ≈Tc + 10K above the superconducting dome around x ≈0.1. Our results
imply an avoided quantum critical point, which is expected to strongly inﬂuence
the properties of both the normal and superconducting state [Phys. Rev. Lett. 110,
257001 (2013)]. Given the signiﬁcant effect of the incommensurate order on super-
conductivity, we use various techniques with different energy resolutions ranging
from μeV to meV to reveal its cluster spin glass nature [Phys. Rev. B 90, 024509
(2014)].
(2) (Chapter6) Neutron scattering study of uniaxial-pressure detwinned
BaFe2−xNixAs2. In this work, nematic spin correlations in the uniaxial-strained
tetragonal of BaFe2−xNixAs2 have been revealed by inelastic neutron scattering. In
the paramagnetic tetragonal phase, an in-plane resistivity anisotropy was observed
in the BaFe2−xTxAs2 (T =Co, Ni) iron pnictides. Inelastic neutron scattering is used
to show that low-energy spin excitations in these materials change from four-fold
symmetry to two-fold symmetry at temperatures corresponding to the onset of the
in-plane resistivity anisotropy. Since both resistivity and spin excitation anisotropy
vanishes near optimal superconductivity, we conclude that the nematic spin corre-
lations is intimately connected to the resistivity anisotropy or they have the same
origin [Science 345, 657–660 (2014)].
(3) (Chapter7) Magnetic spin excitation spectra in overdoped regime have been
studied by time-of-ﬂight neutron scattering. Previous results for parent compound
(x = 0) and optimal doping (x = 0.1) show that the spin excitations below
100 meV are strongly modiﬁed by doping while the higher energy part does not
changed. We studied the evolution of the spin excitations in overdoped regime
(x = 0.15, 0.18, 0.3). The intensity of the spin resonance mode decreases linearly
with doping. The low-energy spin excitations also decrease with doping and form an
∼50meV spin gap in x = 0.3, while the high energy spin excitations evolve slowly.

1.2 Scope of This Thesis
5
Our results suggest that both high-energy spin excitations and low-energy spin exci-
tation coupled with itinerant electrons are important for the superconductivity in iron
pnictides [Phys. Rev. B 88, 144516 (2013). Nat. Commun. 4, 2874 (2013)].
We provide an introduction for iron-based superconductivity in Chap.2 and prin-
ciples and techniques of neutron scattering in Chap.3. In the end of this thesis, we
summarize our research and give an outlook for future studies of iron-based super-
conductors.

Chapter 2
Iron-Based Superconductors
Since Hideo Hosono discovered electron-doped iron-pnictide superconductor
La[O1−xFx]FeAs [1], scientists discovered various iron-pnictide superconductors
containing FeAs layers and iron-selenide superconductors consisting of FeSe (and
interstitial) layers. In the July of 2008, Johrendt group in the University of Munich
suggested that BaFe2As2 in ThCr2Si2 structure could be a parent compound of iron-
based superconductivity [2], and later discovered an optimal superconductivity with
Tc = 38 K in potassium doped BaFe2As2 [3]. Meanwhile, Jin’s group in the Institute
of Physics in Beijing revealed Tc = 18 K superconductivity in LiFeAs [4]. Wu’s
group in Taiwan discovered the superconducting system based on FeSe [5]. After-
wards, superconductivity was achieved via carrier- or isovalent-doping in these sys-
tems [6–11]. According to the stoichiometric ratios of their chemical constituents,
the main families of iron-based superconductors described above are denoted by
‘1111’, ‘122’, ‘111’ and ‘11’, respectively. Iron-based superconductors are the sec-
ond class of high-temperature superconductors after cuprates. Their charge-carrier
dopingphasediagramsaresimilartothoseforcuprates,whiletheirparentcompounds
are poor metals instead of Mott insulators (Fig.2.1). The comparison between iron-
based and copper-oxide superconductors will provide new methods and insights for
understanding high-Tc superconductivity [12]. In the studies of iron-based supercon-
ductors, many universal features among different families have been revealed. In this
chapter, we will introduce some of these universal features that are associated with
the studies of this thesis.
Figure2.1 are schematic phase diagrams of copper-oxide and iron-based high-
temperature superconductors [12]. Iron-based superconductivity is usually induced
by doping charge carriers into its antiferromagnetic (AF) parent compound, which
can be realized via element substitution in FeAs or its adjacent layer in various
systems such as La[O1−xFx]FeAs [1], Ba1−xKxFe2As2 [3], BaFe2−xTxAs2 (T = Co,
Ni) [6, 7], NaFe1−xCoxAs [8]. Superconductivity can also be induced by isovalent
doping such as replacing Fe by Ru in BaFe2−xRuxAs2 [9], As by P in (Eu/Ba)Fe2-
(As1−xPx)2 [10, 11] and Se by Te in Fe(Se,Te) [5]. The doping gradually suppresses

the structural and magnetic phase transitions and induces optimal superconductivity
at the border of structural and AF instability (Fig.2.1). Besides doping, applying
hydrostatic pressure is also efﬁcient in inducing or enhancing superconductivity
in some systems [13]. Thin ﬁlms of some systems also exhibit superconductivity
enhancement [14].
2.1
Basic Properties
2.1.1
Structure and Magnetic Structure
As shown in Fig.2.2, iron-pnictide superconductors with layered structure consist of
FeAs layers and interstitial layers, while iron-selenide superconductors are formed
by stacked FeSe layers [15]. Within the FeX layer (X = As/P, Se/Te/S), four X ions
surround one Fe2+ and form a tetrahedron (Fig.2.2e). The geometry of the FeX4 tetra-
hedron dominates various electronic properties of iron-based superconductors [16].
Parent compound of iron pnictides usually undergoes a tetragonal-to-orthorhombic
structural transition (Ts), and a subsequent (or simultaneous) paramagnetic-to-AF
transition (TN). The tetragonal and orthorhombic unit cells of iron pnictides (122,
111, 1111 systems) are shown in Fig.2.2f, where the orthorhombic unit cell are
rotated by 45◦and enlarged relative to the tetragonal unit cell (ao ≈bo ≈
√
2aT ).
The magnetic ground state is a colinear stripe order (C-type) with magnetic wave
vector k = (1, 0, 1)ort = (1/2, 1/2, 1)tet [17–22]. Compared with iron pnictides, iron
selenides show different structural and magnetic transitions. Fe1+yTe exhibit a simul-
taneous tetragonal-to-monoclinic structural and magnetic transition (Ts = TN ≈70

Since we focus on the studies of BaFe2−xNixAs2, we now turn to discuss the
magnetic and structural properties of BaFe2As2 in detail. At high temperature,

12
2
Iron-Based Superconductors
BaFe2As2 shows tetragonal structure with space group I4/mmm and lattice para-
meters a = b = 3.963 Å, c = 13.02 Å, as shown in Fig.2.2. As cooling,
BaFe2As2 undergoes a structural transition and a subsequent magnetic transition
(Ts ≈TN + 0.2 K ≈138 K) [24]. The system enters an orthorhombic phase below
Ts and then an AF state below TN, with space group Fmmm and lattice parame-
ters a = 5.615 Å, b = 5.574 Å, c = 12.95 Å at base temperature. The AF order
of BaFe2As2 is a C-type colinear stripe order with magnetic vector k = (1, 0, 1)
in the orthorhombic notation and (1/2, 1/2, 1) in the tetragonal notation (Fig.2.2).
Relative to the tetragonal state, the orthorhombic unit cell are rotated by 45◦with
ao =
√
2aT . The other parent compounds of the 122 system such as CaFe2As2,
SrFe2As2 and EuFe2As2 have similar structural and magnetic properties [22, 25],
though they show different lattice parameters, magnetic moments and structural
and magnetic transition temperatures. The main difference among these 122 parent
compounds is the order of the structural and the magnetic phase transitions [26].
BaFe2As2 exhibits second-order structural transition, as indicated by the gradual
onset of the orthorhombic lattice distortion δ = (ao −bo)/(ao +bo) in Fig.2.3a [24],
and ﬁrst-order magnetic transition that induces a hysteresis and a jump of δ via strong
magnetoelastic coupling (Fig.2.3a). With increasing electron doping, the tempera-
ture difference between Ts and TN is enlarged and the magnetic transition becomes
second order (Fig.2.3b) [24]. The others, however, show simultaneous ﬁrst-order
structural and magnetic phase transitions in undoped and underdoped regime [21,
22, 25, 26]. The order of the structural and magnetic phase transitions, and whether
they occur simultaneously, have signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the electronic properties
close to the transitions.
In the tetragonal state, electronic properties within ab plane of the doped
BaFe2As2 systems show C4 symmetry, as dictated by the structural symmetry and
revealed by experimental studies. Similarly, samples in the orthorhombic phase

2.1 Basic Properties
13
should exhibit two-fold (C2) electronic properties. However, the tetragonal-to-
orthorhombic structural transition results in twinning, as shown in Fig.2.3c. Four
kinds of domains formed with their ao axis aligned along four different directions
below Ts and hinder the measurements of the intrinsic electronic properties along ao
and bo axes [27]. To measure the intrinsic electronic properties, the ﬁrst step is to
obtain a detwinned, single domain crystal. Tanatar et al. in Ames Lab [28] and Chu
et al. in Stanford university [29] independently developed mechanical detwinning
devices that can apply uniaxial stress for crystal detwinning. Their resistivity mea-
surements on BaFe2As2 and BaFe2−xTxAs2 (T =Co, Ni) revealed strong resistivity
anisotropy between ρa and ρb [29, 30]. ρa is smaller and shows metallic behavior
below a characteristic temperature T ∗, while ρb behaves like an insulator below T ∗.
T ∗is much higher than Ts, indicating that this electronic anisotropy persists in the
tetragonal state. The electronic anisotropy in the tetragonal state of uniaxial-pressure
detwinned Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 is a solid evidence for the emergence of an electronic
nematic in iron pnictides [29]. Similar electronic anisotropy in the tetragonal state
has also been observed in uniaxial-strained BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 but absent (or much
weaker) in Ba1−xKxFe2As2, suggesting that it is easier to induce such anisotropy
by doping FeAs layer [31, 32]. In addition, resistivity measurements on detwinned
CaFe2As2 and SrFe2As2 reveal vanishingly small resistivity anisotropy above Ts
and therefore suggested that the electronic nematic is sensitive to the order of the
structural transition [28, 33]. To study the electronic nematic in iron pnictides, we
use neutron scattering to study the magnetic order and spin excitations in uniaxial-
strained BaFe2−xNixAs2. We shall discuss the details in Chap.6.
The strong electronic anisotropy in the tetragonal state of uniaxial-strained Ba-
(Fe1−xCox)2As2 implies that the electronic properties of underdoped iron pnictides
are susceptible to structural changes. Indeed, various studies have suggested that the
electronic properties of iron pnictides are sensitive to the distortions of the FeAs4
tetrahedron. To be speciﬁc, the anion height (AH) and the bond angle α of the FeAs4
octahedron (Fig.2.2) are correlated with the superconductivity in iron pnictides. As
shown in Fig.2.4a, Mizuguchi et al. summarized AH and Tc for many iron-based
superconductors. As increasing AH, Tc ﬁrstly increases and then decreases, forming a
peak at AH = 1.38 Å [34]. Subsequently, Lee et al. plotted the relationship between
Tc and α, as displayed in Fig.2.4b. This plot suggests that a system containing
undistorted FeAs4 tetrahedron (α = 109.47◦) exhibits higher Tc [35]. The structural
effects above suggested that superconductivity can be induced by charge carrier
doping as well as chemical pressure generated by element substitution. On the other
hand, element substitution would induce impurity scattering, which is detrimental
to superconductivity. Therefore, the emergence of superconductivity is driven and
affected by various factors and cannot be simply explained by charge carrier doping.

2.1.2
Electronic Structure
Because of the common FeAs layer, iron pnictides exhibit similar Fermi surface
topology. Compared with the single-band copper oxides, iron based superconductors
are multi-band systems. Their Fermi surfaces usually consist of hole pockets around
 point and electron pockets around M(X) point.
Figure2.5 shows the Fermi surface and band structure of LaOFeAs and LaOFeP
calculatedbyLDAandmeasuredbyangle-resolvedphotoemission(ARPES),respec-
tively [36–38]. The Fermi surface of LaOFeAs includes two hole pockets at the center
of the Brillouin zone () and two electron pockets at zone corner (Fig.2.5a). Some
of the Fermi pockets show strong dispersion along kz while the others are more two
dimensional (Fig.2.5b). Since the hole pockets at  show similar size to the electron
pockets at M, Fermi surface nesting between them could occur with nesting vector
k = (1/2, 1/2)tet = (1, 0)ort [36]. As suggested by theoretical calculation, the per-
fect Fermi surface nesting tends to induce an AF spin density wave ground state with
wave vector k = (1/2, 1/2)tet [36]. Subsequently, neutron diffraction measurements
on LaOFeAs conﬁrm the existence of the AF order in LaOFeAs [17]. Figure2.5c
depicts the contributions of different orbitals to the total electron density of states of
LaOFeAs. The density of states close to Fermi surface are dominated by the orbitals
of iron, while the p orbitals of As lie 3 eV below the Fermi surface. Therefore, the
electronic properties of the system depends on the electron hopping between Fe ions.
Figure2.5d are band structures along high symmetry directions. The blue and green
dashed curves are the band structures for 3.5 pm larger and smaller anion heights,
respectively. Note the anion height for LaOFeAs is 130 pm. The change of the anion
height induces similar band shift to that caused by charge carrier doping. As we have
discussed in last section, the anion height has signiﬁcant effects on the electronic

electron correlations. However, as we shall discuss later, the real situation is more
complicated.
As suggested by LDA calculation, 122 system exhibits similar Fermi surface
topology and hosts two hole pockets at  point and two electron pockets at tetragonal
zone corner (X point) [39]. As increasing charge carrier doping, the Fermi level shifts
gradually, which changes the Fermi surface nesting condition. LDA calculation also
suggests that the anion height decreases with hole doping. This can be ascribed to
the enhanced attraction between Fe2+ and As3−after the injection of holes into the
Fe2+ sites and the decrease of the radius of Fe ions after hole injection.
2.2
Phase Diagrams
In this section, we will describe the phase diagrams of doped BaFe2As2 systems and
discuss the emergence of the AF order, the orthorhombic lattice distortions and the
superconductivity, and the interplay between these orders.

16
2
Iron-Based Superconductors
Fig. 2.6 a Hole- and b electron-doping phase diagrams of doped BaFe2As2 system [40, 41]. The
AFM, Ort, Tet, SC are antiferromagnetic order, orthorhombic, tetragonal and superconductivity,
respectively. The Ts, TN and Tc are structural, antiferromagnetic and superconducting transition
temperatures. The left panel is reprinted with permission from [40]. Copyright 2012 by the American
Physical Society. The right panel is reprinted with permission from [41]. Copyright 2010 by the
American Physical Society
Figure2.6 shows the phase diagrams for hole- and electron-doped BaFe2As2. In
Ba1−xKxFe2As2, the substitution of Ba by potassium induces holes into the FeAs
layers indirectly. The superconductivity emerges at x = 0.1 and persists to the end
member x = 1, with optimal superconductivity (Tc = 39 K) achieved at x =
0.4 [40]. Compared with Ba1−xKxFe2As2, electron-doped Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 are
distinct in various aspects [41]. The element substitution occurs at the Fe sites and
induces electron doping in FeAs layers directly. Since the structure of FeAs layer
dominates many electronic properties of iron pnictides, introducing charge carrier by
element substitution in or out-of-FeAs layers are very different. As shown in Fig.2.6,
superconductivity in Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 appears from x = 0.03 and persists only
before x = 0.13 with optimal Tc = 26 K occurred at x = 0.062, and the AF order is
suppressed more quickly than that in Ba1−xKxFe2As2. This substantial electron-hole
asymmetry reﬂects different electron correlation in these two sides. The microscopic
origin, however, remains elusive.
In doped 122 systems, charge carrier doping in the interstitial layers adjacent to
the FeAs layers are more effective in inducing superconductivity, for which opti-
mal Tc is higher than 30 K in hole-doped Ba1−xKxFe2As2 and Ba1−xNaxFe2As2
and higher than 40 K in electron-doped Ca1−xLaxFe2As2. However, hole doping
in FeAs layer fails to induce superconductivity because Cr and Mn that suppose to
provide holes have strong magnetism and induce strong impurity scattering, which
are detrimental to superconductivity. The electron- and isovalent-doping in FeAs
layer, such as substituting Fe by Co, Ni, Ru and As by P, can induce superconduc-
tivity exhibiting optimal Tc ∼20−30 K [6, 7, 9, 11]. With increasing doping, the
AF order and orthorhombic state are gradually suppressed. The structural and the
magnetic transitions are coupled ﬁrst-order transition in Ba1−xKxFe2As2 and BaFe2-

2.2 Phase Diagrams
17
(As1−xPx)2, while Ts and TN split with increasing doping in Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 and
BaFe2−xNixAs2.
2.2.1
Spin Density Wave
Since the parent compounds of iron pnictides show poor metallic ground state, and
their Fermi surface topology could allow Fermi surface nesting between the hole
pockets at  point and the electron pockets at M point, Dong et al. proposed that a spin
density wave (SDW) state could be formed in LaOFeAs, driven by the Fermi surface
nesting (Fig.2.5a) [36]. SDW is a symmetry-breaking state induced by the instability
of Fermi surface in an itinerant magnetic system [42]. An AF order corresponding
to the Fermi surface nesting was indeed discovered by neutron diffraction (Fig.2.2)
[17]. Because of the important role of Fermi surface nesting and itinerant magnetism
in creating the AF groundstate and driving the magnetic dynamics of iron pnictides,
it is necessary to introduce the basic ideas of Fermi-surface-nesting driven SDW.
As shown in Fig.2.7a, the Fermi surface of one-dimensional electron gas are two
points at ±kF. This special Fermi surface is susceptible to small perturbations, much
different from its counterparts in higher dimensions. From linear response theory,
electron density distribution caused by an external perturbation φ(q) can be expressed
as:
ρind(q) = χ(q)φ(q),
(2.1)
where χ(q) is the Lindhard Response Function of the system. The response function
in d dimensions can be written as
χ(q) =

dk
(2π)d
fk −fk+q
Ek −Ek+q
,
(2.2)
in which fk = f (εk) is Fermi distribution function. For the scattering between the
occupied state and the unoccupied state in one dimension (Fig.2.7a), fk −fk+q ≡1
while Ek −Ek+q can be inﬁnitesimal close to q = 2kF, resulting in a divergence of
the response function at q = 2kF, as shown in Fig.2.7b. This divergence corresponds
to an unstable state, which is usually avoided by a periodic distribution of the electron
(orspin)densitywithwavevectorq = 2kF,thatis,densitywave.Theformationofthe
q = 2kF density wave folds the band structure and open a density-wave gap, leaving
the system into a stable state [43]. Figure2.7c shows the band folding and energy
gap formation for a system with lattice parameter a. The period of the density wave
is 2a, corresponding to a smaller Brillouin zone π
a . Therefore, the periodicity of the
density wave induces an energy gap at ± π
2a [43]. For higher dimensions d = 2, 3,
only one q point on the Fermi surface meets the nesting condition, which cannot
result in any divergence in the corresponding response function Eq.2.2, as shown in
Figs.2.7b, d.

Fig. 2.7 a Schematic diagram of Fermi surface nesting in one-dimensional electron gas. b q
dependence of the response function χq in dimensions d = 1, 2, 3. c Upper Spin density wave
in real space. Lower Band structure of one-dimensional spin density wave. d Schematic of Fermi
surface nesting in two-dimensional electron gas, in which only one q point meets the nesting
condition. [42]
Free electron gas is a single-band system. Therefore, Fermi surface nesting cannot
be realized in two and three dimensions. In the multiband iron pnictides, the interband
scattering between the hole Fermi surface at  point and the electron Fermi surface at
M(X) point could induce Fermi surface instability and result in a SDW state with AF
wave vector (1/2, 1/2) 2π
aT (Fig.2.2). Indeed, infrared optical measurements observed
spectral weight transfer across the Néel temperature TN in LaOFeAs, indicating an
energy-gapopeningbelow TN,inagreementwiththeSDWpicture[36, 44].However,
ARPES measurements revealed Fermi surface reconstruction instead of energy gap
opening in iron pnictides [45, 46]. Therefore, whether the AF order is a SDW state
remains an open question. Furthermore, the parent compound of 11 system (FeTe) has
similar Fermi surface topology to iron pnictides but shows bicolinear AF order with
wave vector k = (1/2, 0)( 2π
aT ) Fig.2.2, suggesting that the AF order in iron-based
superconductors cannot be fully described by the SDW picture [47]. In addition, the
magnetic moment for FeTe, ∼2μB/Fe, is much larger than those in iron pnictides such
as 0.35 μB in LaOFeAs [17], 0.87 μB in BaFe2As2 [18] and ∼0.1μB in Na1−δFeAs
[19], indicative of a local-moment picture. If the AF orders in these four families of

2.2 Phase Diagrams
19
iron-based superconductors share the same origin, a comprehensive picture beyond
SDW is required, for instance, theoretical attempts emphasizing correlation effects
and Hund’s coupling have been proposed [48, 49].
2.2.2
Superconductivity and Fermi Surface Nesting
In doped BaFe2As2 systems, it is well known that charge carrier doping gradually
suppresses the long range AF order and induces optimal superconductivity at the
boarder of the AF instability [40, 41]. In the view of band structure, charge carrier
doping will change the chemical potential of the system and shift the Fermi surface.
The band shift will change the sizes of the Fermi pockets and therefore vary the
nesting condition [50–53]. In the theory of Fermi surface nesting, minor change of
Fermi surface sizes can greatly change the nesting condition and suppress the AF
order. Although the perfect nesting inducing the AF order will be broken, interband
scattering between partial Fermi surface of the hole pockets and the electron pockets
can persist in superconducting regime. Since this partial nesting could enhance AF
spin ﬂuctuations which could be a driving force for superconductivity, Fermi surface
nesting has also been connected to the evolution of the superconductivity in these
systems. As shown in Fig.2.8a, b, in Ba1−xKxFe2As2, Fermi surface nesting between
hole band α and electron bands ϵ and γ are enhanced in x = 0.25 and 0.4 sample,
which was suggested to enhance the interband scattering (and spin ﬂuctuations)
and therefore improve superconductivity (Tc). As increasing hole doping to x = 0.7,
nestingconditionsbecomeworseand Tc decreasesto22K.TheendmemberKFe2As2
(x = 1) shows only Tc = 3 K, where the γ pocket disappears and the ϵ band
changes a lot in topology, both of which suppress the nesting condition. Similar
doping evolution of Fermi surface nesting and superconductivity was also observed in
electron-dopedBa(Fe1−xCox)2As2.Thisconsistencyinthedopingevolutionbetween
nesting and superconductivity in various systems indicates that Fermi surface nesting
is intimately connected to the superconductivity in iron pnictides.
Since low energy spin ﬂuctuations, including the spin resonance mode, have been
suggested to arise from the interband scattering between the hole pockets at  and
electron pockets at M(X), better Fermi surface nesting condition means stronger
spin ﬂuctuations, which could enhance superconductivity if the superconductivity
is indeed driven by spin ﬂuctuations. The spin resonance mode was proposed to
be an important evidence of the s±-wave superconductivity in iron-based super-
conductors mediated by spin ﬂuctuations [54]. Indeed, the spin resonance mode was
subsequentlydiscoveredinvariousiron-basedsuperconductorsandstronglysupports
the s± model. The s± wave symmetry were also supported by the superconducting
gaps in various pnictide superconductors measured by ARPES [55–58]. Scanning
tunneling microscope measurements of the superconducting gap and the quasiparti-
cle interference under magnetic ﬁeld in Fe(Se,Te) also favor the s± wave symmetry
[59].

Although Fermi surface nesting was suggested to be essential for the spin ﬂuctua-
tions and the superconductivity in iron-based superconductors, experimental studies
for their connections, and their doping evolution, are still lacking. One of our main
goals is to explore the doping evolution of the Fermi surface, spin ﬂuctuations and
superconductivity, as well as their connections, in electron-doped BaFe2−xNixAs2,
as we shall discuss in Chap.7.

2.2 Phase Diagrams
21
2.2.3
Coexisting and Competing Orders
The coexistence and competition between various orders such as AF order,
orthorhombic state and superconductivity are crucial for understanding the micro-
scopic mechanism of the superconductivity in iron-based superconductors. Studies
of the interplay between these orders can reveal their connections and provide an
experimental basis for developing a comprehensive theoretical model.
As we have discussed, underdoped BaFe2As2 samples undergo a structural phase
transition (Ts = 138 K, δ = 0.36% for BaFe2As2) and a magnetic phase transi-
tion (TN = 138 K, m = 0.87μB for BaFe2As2). In the underdoped regime, with
increasing doping, the structural transition and the magnetic transition are gradu-
ally suppressed. The transition temperatrues (Ts and TN), the orthorhombic lattice
distortions (δ) and magnetic moment decrease monotonically with increasing dop-
ing. Meanwhile, superconductivity emerges and reach its maximum at the border of
the structural and magnetic instability. The contrasting trends suggest a competition
between superconductivity and orthorhombic AF state, and a coupling between the
orthorhombic phase and the AF order. This interplay was an indirect speculation at
early time, but became clear in the phase diagrams determined by transport, mag-
netic, neutron scattering and x-ray diffraction measurements in various systems. The
underdoped region hosting orthorhombic state, AF order and superconductivity is of
particular interest in the studies of the correlations between these orders. Figure2.6
shows the research progress concerning this issue [40, 41].
When superconductivity emerges from the orthorhombic AF ground state, it has
low Tc and small superconducting volume fraction. The interplay between these
ordersarenotobvious.AsincreasingdopingtoweakentheorthorhombicAFstateand
enhance the superconductivity, a suppression of the orthorhombic lattice distortion
(δ) and the AF order parameter occurs below Tc, as shown in Fig.2.9a–c. In Fig.2.9a–
b, orthorhombic lattice distortions δ in Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 are heavily suppressed
below Tc. In the optimal doping (x = 0.063), the system even re-enters the tetragonal
state, as dictated by the merge of the two orthorhombic peaks at 8.3 K in Fig.2.9a
[41]. Figure2.9c shows the split Ts and TN and the suppression of the magnetic order
parameter below Tc in x = 0.047 of Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2[60]. The suppression of the
AF state becomes stronger at higher doping. In a doping close to optimal doping, the
AF order parameter is almost completely suppressed at a temperature below Tc [41,
60, 62, 63].
The competition between superconductivity and AF order, and the strong mag-
netoelastic coupling have been discovered not only in many electron doped systems
such as Co, Ni, Rh doped BaFe2As2 [64–67], but also in hole-doped Ba1−xKxFe2-
As2 [40] and isovalent-doped Ba(Fe1−xRux)2As2 [68] and BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 [69,
70]. These experimental facts indicate that the interplay is universal in iron pnic-
tides. Thus far, we note that the systems showing weak impurity scattering, such
as BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 and Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2, exhibit strong suppression of the AF
order, while some systems like BaFe2−xNixAs2 hosting stronger impurity scattering,
show weaker suppression.

These unusual correlations have been interpreted by the competition between
superconductivity and AF (SDW) state (since they share the same itinerant electrons)
and the strong elastomagnetic coupling [63]. This also means that the superconduc-
tivity favors a paramagnetic tetragonal state, which is more isotropic (symmetric)
than the orthorhombic AF state. This is an important point that must be taken into
account in establishing a theoretical model for explaining the phase diagrams of iron
pnictides.

2.2 Phase Diagrams
23
Besides the competition, whether the AF order coexist with superconductivity
in a microscopic scale is also an important question which is still controversial.
Generally, it is difﬁcult to judge the scale of this coexistence, since both microscopic
andmesoscopiccoexistencecandescribetheexperimental observations fromneutron
scattering and x-ray diffraction. Some local probes such as muon spin rotation (μSR)
and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) support the microscopic coexistence of AF
order and superconductivity. In a NMR study of Ba(Fe1−xRux)2As2 [71], Ma et
al. found that magnetic volume fraction for underdoped samples is 100% below
TN and the magnetic signal falls sharply below Tc, providing a solid evidence for
the microscopic coexistence of AF order and superconductivity. Similar conclusion
was drawn in μSR measurements of Ba1−xKxFe2As2 [61]. In underdoped Ba1−x-
KxFe2As2 hosting bulk superconductivity, the magnetic volume fraction below Tc is
100%, suggesting that AF order and the superconductivity coexist in a microscopic
scale [61]. Complete understanding concerning this issue is still lacking and remains
to be explored experimentally and theoretically. In this thesis, one important work
is to clarify the correlations between the AF order, the lattice distortion and the
superconductivity close to optimal doping of BaFe2−xNixAs2, as we shall discuss in
Chaps.4 and 5.

Chapter 3
Neutron Scattering
3.1
Historical Overview
Neutron is a charge-neutral particle discovered in 1932 by James Chadwick, who
won the Nobel prize in physics for this discovery in 1935. Neutron is an subatomic
particle with zero charge and slightly larger mass than proton (mn = 1.674927471 ×
1027 kg). It has a spin of 1/2 and magnetic moment μn = γ μNσn, where γ = −1.913
is geometric ratio and μN = e¯h/(2m p) is nuclear magneton. Therefore, the mag-
netic moment of neutron is 1.913μN, or 1.0419 × 10−3μB. Because of its magnetic
moment, neutron can interact with the magnetic ﬁeld of unpaired electrons in mate-
rials and probe the magnetic information. Without charge, neutron is a strong pene-
trating radiation and can probe bulk information of materials without being affected
by surface effects in measurements. This is a unique advantage compared with x-ray
scattering. In addition, neutron has a mean lifetime of 886s, which is enough for
measurements.
According to the wave-particle duality of neutron, we can easily get its energy-
momentum relation. Fortunately, the wavelength of thermal neutron (∼Å) matches
the lattice spacings of condensed matter systems. With magnetic moment, neutron
is an ideal tool for probing structure and magnetic structure of condensed matter
systems. More importantly, the momenta and energies of cold neutron and thermal
neutron are similar to that for various elementary excitations such as phonon and
magnetic excitations, making neutron scattering a powerful probe of elementary
excitations in solids. With respect to its energy, neutron can be classiﬁed as: cold
neutron (0.1–10meV), thermal neutron (10–100meV), hot neutron (100–500meV)
and epithermal neutron (>500meV). The interactions between neutron and matter
are very weak. The corresponding theoretical analysis are therefore simple [1].
Because of the wave-particle duality of neutron and the development of electron
diffraction technique, neutron diffraction was demonstrated to be feasible in principle
by von Halban in 1936. However, neutron diffraction was not developed to be a
practical experimental method due to the low ﬂux of the neutron sources at that time.
In 1938, German physicist Lise Meitner and Otto Hahn discovered nuclear ﬁssion.
© Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2017
X. Lu, Phase Diagram and Magnetic Excitations of BaFe2−xNixAs2: A Neutron
Scattering Study, Springer Theses, DOI 10.1007/978-981-10-4998-9_3
29

30
3
Neutron Scattering
In 1944, Fermi and his colleagues built the ﬁrst nuclear reactor, in which the nuclear
chain reaction can generate high ﬂux neutron beam. Neutron scattering studies were
performed since powerful nuclear reactors were built, such as the reactors in Oak
Ridge National Laboratory in United States and Chalk River Laboratory in Canada.
In Oak Ridge, Ernest Wollan and Clifford G. Shull developed a two-axis neutron
diffractometer since 1946 and applied it to structural determination of crystal. They
observed the antiferromagnetic Bragg peak of MnO crystal and demonstrated the
existence of antiferromagnetic order. In the ﬁrst several years of neutron scattering,
most studies focused on diffraction measurements. In 1952, Bertram Brockhouse
in Chalk River Laboratory invented the triple-axis spectrometer that can be used
to do inelastic neutron scattering. Triple-axis spectrometer were used to measure
phonon dispersions of some crystals and showed that inelastic neutron scattering is
a powerful spectroscopy method. Nowadays, neutron triple-axis spectrometer is still
an important tool for studying lattice and spin dynamics of solids. In 1994, Clifford
G. Shull and Bertram Brockhouse were awarded the Nobel prize in physics for their
original contributions in applying neutron scattering to the studies of condensed
matter physics [2].
Inspired by the ﬁrst neutron scattering studies performed by Wollan et al., neutron
scattering techniques were developed quickly. Several nuclear reactors designed for
neutron scattering were built since then. The ﬁrst reactor for neutron scattering is
the HFBR (High-ﬂux beam reactor) in Brookhaven National Laboratory in United
States. Later, Insitut Laue-Langevin was built in 1971 and is an international neu-
tron scattering center nowadays. Similar neutron sources include Orphée in France
(1980), FRM II (MLZ) in Germany (2003), OPAL in Austrilia, JRRR in Japan,
CARR in China. Meanwhile, pulsed neutron spallation sources were also devel-
oped. The time-of-ﬂight technique and the accessibility of high energy neutrons in
pulsed spallation neutron source make it indispensable for neutron scattering studies
of condensed matter systems. The operating spallation neutron sources around the
world include ISIS at Rutherford-Appleton Laboratory in United Kingdom, SNS
at Oak Ridge National Laboratory in United States and J-PARC in Japan. With
rapid development of condensed matter physics, more advanced neutron sources and
instruments are required. The Chinese Spallation Neutron Source (CSNS) and the
European Spallation Source (ESS) in construction will play important roles in future
neutron scattering studies [2].
3.2
Principles
Neutron scattering is a well developed experimental technique. The related theoret-
ical principles have been well descripted in several text books [2–4]. In this section,
we are going to introduce the principles of neutron scattering associated with our
studies.

scattered because of the interactions between neutron and the sample. The energy-
momentum distribution of the scattered neutrons are analyzed and detected by spe-

3.2 Principles
33
3.2.2
Nuclear Scattering
Since the range of nuclear force is much smaller than the wavelength of thermal
neutron, the scattering is dominated by s wave (l = 0). For the scattering involving
a single nucleus, the scattering amplitude can be denoted by a complex parameter b
(scattering length). The imaginary part of b describes neutron absorption (absorption
cross section). The total cross section is the sum of the scattering cross section and
the absorption cross section:
σtot = σs + σa = 4π|b|2 + 4π
ki
Im(b)
(3.8)
Since absorption cross section in most elements are quite small, we neglect it in most
cases and get:
σtot ≃σs = 4π|b|2.
(3.9)
Based on Eq.3.9, the cross sections in our discussions below are all real. Different
isotopes of the same elements usually have different scattering lengthes. The scatter-
ing length depends on the relative direction between the spins of neutron and nucleus
when the net spin of the nucleus is not zero.
Scattering cross section usually contains coherent and incoherent parts. Generally,
the differential cross section for a single nucleus can be expressed as:

respectively. Coherent cross sections usually arise from coherent scattering associ-
ated with lattice, for instance, Bragg scattering and phonon scattering. Incoherent
cross sections come from the interactions between the spins of neutron and nucleus,
which is usually isotropic. The later can be treated as a background and need to be
subtracted before analyzing the coherent scattering signals.
The nuclear coherent scattering depends on the positions of the atoms in sample.
Its cross section is

Since incoherent scattering cross section (σinc) is isotropic and can be easily
calculated from Eq.3.15, material with strong σinc (such as Vanadium) [5] is usually
used as a standard sample to calibrate the magnitude of the elementary excitations
in materials. For example, the cross section of vanadium is dominated by σinc ≈500
barns (σc ≈2 barns). Therefore, the elementary excitations in solids are usually
compared with that of a standard vanadium sample to get its absolute intensity.
We have discussed above the elastic nuclear scattering. The inelastic nuclear
scattering is not described here, which can be referred to Ref. [3].
3.2.3
Magnetic Interactions and Elastic Magnetic Scattering
Magnetic neutron scattering arises from the interactions between the magnetic
moment of neutron and the magnetic ﬁeld generated by magnetic ions (with unpaired
electrons) inside magnetic materials. The interacting potential for magnetic neutron
scattering can be written as
VM(r) = −μn · B(r),
(3.16)
in which μn is the magnetic moment of neutron and B represents the local magnetic
ﬁeld around magnetic ions. Do Fourier transform for Eq.3.16 and use Maxwell’s
equations, we have
VM(Q) = −μn · B(Q) = −μ0μn · M⊥(Q),
(3.17)

3.2 Principles
35
where M⊥(Q) represents the magnetization projected to the direction perpendicular
to the scattering wave vector:
M⊥(Q) = ˆQ × {M( ˆQ) × ˆQ}.
(3.18)
The expression of M⊥(Q) is usually complicated and material dependent. Often-
times, dipole approximation is enough for describing the interacting potential. In 3d
transition-metal ions, the spin-orbit coupled total angular momentum J is not a good
quantum number, because the orbital angular momentum L is usually quenched by
crystal ﬁeld. In this case, the very small residual L along S caused by spin-orbit
coupling can be denoted by (g −2)S. With dipole approximation, the magnetization
can be written as
M(Q) = −gμB f (Q)S = f (Q)μ,
(3.19)
in which, μ = −gμBS and f (Q) are the magnetic moment and the magnetic form
factor of the magnetic ion, respectively. g is Landé factor determined by spin and
orbit angular momenta. g equals 2 for a system without L, while systems where L
is not completely quenched will have a different g value.
In magnetic ordered system, neutron diffraction can probe the magnetic Bragg
peaks corresponding to the magnetic order. A magnetic Bragg peak is an average
of the scattering from the whole sample. The magnetic moment of the ions forming
the magnetic order can be derived from the intensity of the magnetic Bragg peaks.
Correlation length for the magnetic order can be estimated from the width of the
magnetic Bragg peaks. The temperature dependence of the intensity for a magnetic
Bragg peak represents the magnetic order parameter. Similar to elastic nuclear scat-
tering, magnetic Bragg peaks can be observed when the momentum transfer Q is
coincident with the magnetic wave vector in reciprocal space. For example, in long
range antiferromagnetic system such as MnO, since the magnetic unit cell is one
time larger than the structural unit cell, the magnetic Bragg peaks can be observed
at half integers in reciprocal lattice.
In dipole approximation, magnetic elastic scattering cross section can be written

3.2.5
Dynamic Structure Factor S(Q, ω) and Generalized
Susceptibility χ′′(Q, ω)
The dynamic structure factor S(Q, ω) of a system contains all the information of the
magnetic dynamics such as dispersion and spectral weight distribution. Meanwhile,
it can be calculated from linear response theory based on the magnetic properties
of the studied system. Therefore, S(Q, ω) can bridge the experimental observations
and theoretical scenarios, which is signiﬁcant for understanding the spin dynamics
of a magnetic system [6–8].
In a linear system, the response (R(x, t)) induced by a small external perturbation
(H(x, t)) is proportional to the perturbation R(x, t) = χ(x, t) · H(x, t). This linear
relation also holds when the perturbation is weak in a non-linear system. In these
cases, the χ(x, t) between the response and the perturbation is termed response func-
tion (or generalized susceptibility), which describes the susceptibility of a system
to external perturbations. A simple example of susceptibility is the static magnetic
susceptibility. In the measurements of magnetic dynamics, the interaction between
neutron and sample is a perturbation. The magnetic dynamics excited by the inter-
action is the response to the perturbation and therefore can be described by linear
response theory. If the response function χ(Q, ω) of a spin system is known, it will
be straightforward to get its response to external perturbation H(Q, ω) by χ · H.
In order to compare the scattering function Sα,β(Q, ω) with theoretical calculation
results, we need to introduce the corresponding susceptibility χ′′
α,β(Q, ω). General-
ized susceptibility is a complex function (χ(Q, ω) = χ′(Q, ω) + iχ′′(Q, ω)) which
can be calculated from linear response theory [7]. The real part χ′(Q, ω) describe
the in-phase response and the imaginary part χ′′(Q, ω) is the out-of-phase response
which describes the energy dissipation of the system caused by external perturbation.
Assuming the imaginary part will be zero while ω approaches inﬁnity, the real part
and the imaginary part can be connected via the Krammers-Kroning relations
χ′αβ = −2ω
π p
 ∞
0
χ′(Q, ω′)
ω′2 −ω2 dω′,
(3.26)
in which p means taking the principal value of the integral.
The dynamic structural factor S(Q, ω) and the imaginary part of the generalized
susceptibility χα,β(Q, ω) can be connected via the ﬂuctuation-dissipation theorem,
Sαβ(Q, ω) = {n(ω) + 1} 1
π χ′′αβ(Q, ω),
(3.27)
where
n(ω) =
1
eℏω/kBT −1.
(3.28)

38
3
Neutron Scattering
is Bose factor. Thus, the dynamic correlation function Sαβ(Q, ω) determines the
magnetic dynamics of the studied system. Detailed discussions about the ﬂuctuation-
dissipation theorem and its applications in magnetic materials can be referred to Ref.
[7].
From detailed balance theorem, an important relation about the dynamic correla-
tion function can be derived:
Sαβ(Q, ω) = eℏω/kBT Sαβ(−Q, −ω).
(3.29)
It means that the neutron energy loss processes (ω > 0) will dominate when the
system is at low temperature (close to groundstate), while the neutron energy gain
processes will become important at high temperature, where the thermal-excited
elementary excitations can give energy to the scattered neutrons.
The determination of the χ′′(Q, ω) in iron pnictides will be discussed in Chap.7.
3.3
Neutron Scattering Spectrometry
Neutron scattering plays an important role in the studies of condensed matter systems.
Various neutron scattering techniques have been widely used in many ﬁelds such
as biological physics, physical chemistry, and solid state physics. In this thesis,
we focus on the studies of the magnetic order and spin dynamics in iron-based
superconductors. Here we introduce the principles of two kinds of spectrometers,
triple-axis spectrometer and time-of-ﬂight spectrometer, which were used in our
studies.
3.3.1
Triple-Axis Spectrometry
Triple-axis spectrometer (TAS) is the earliest neutron spectrometer designed for
probing elementary excitations in solids, and still an important tool nowadays. TAS
usually has high resolution (0.1–2meV) and can reach every point in the covered
(Q, ω) space. A variety of sample environments are compatible with TAS, make it
very ﬂexible for many research purposes. However, TAS also has some disadvan-
tages: Its coverage in (Q, ω) space is relative small (ℏω < 100meV); measurements
using TAS are usually performed point by point (or multi-points) in the (Q, ω) space,
suffering from low measuring efﬁciency.
There are many textbooks and references concerning the principle and instru-
mentation of TAS [2, 9, 10]. In this section, we will emphasize the instrumentation
and introduce some basic principles. Figure3.2 depicts the constituents of a TAS
and its regular experimental setup. A TAS usually consists of monochromator (axis
one), sample table (axis two), analyzer (axis three), collimator and detector. Through
controlling the independent three axes and tuning (ki, k f , 2θ, ψ), TAS can be driven

3.3 Neutron Scattering Spectrometry
39
to any point in the covered (Q, ω) space, by which means Q and ω are decoupled.
Usually, the measurements can only be performed within the horizontal scattering
plane.
3.3.1.1
Constituents of TAS
Thermal neutron beam guided out from a neutron source is usually well moderated by
heavy water etc., and therefore follows Maxwell’s distribution with the intensity max-
imum corresponding to the neutron energy of 25meV. The incident beam will then be
monochromatized by a monochromator and strike a sample. The scattered neutrons
are analyzed by an energy analyzer. Both the monochromator and the analyzer are
usually composed of pyrolitic graphite (PG) crystals and select single-wavelength
neutrons via Bragg reﬂection, by which ki and k f can be tuned continuously. The
Bragg law selecting the incident and exit neutrons are
λi( f ) = 2dM(A)sin(θM(A)),
(3.30)
ki( f ) = 2π
λi( f )
,
(3.31)
Ei( f ) =
ℏ2k2
i( f )
2mn
.
(3.32)
PG crystal has a constant c-axis lattice parameter with the in-plane a and b axes
aligned along different directions in different layers randomly. Its nuclear Bragg peak
(002) (c = 6.7079Å) is usually adopted to select ki and k f in neutron scattering. The
disorder in the ab plane greatly reduces the second-order scattering. The reﬂection
efﬁciency of the (002) Bragg peak is about ∼60–70%. Another advantage of using
PG as monochromator and analyzer is that PG ﬁlter can be applied simultaneously.
The monochromatized neutron beam has a divergence angle, especially after
focusing. The divergence angle broadens the energy and momentum resolution of
TAS. This issue happens to various parts of a spectrometer. Since the scattering plane
is within horizontal plane, it is necessary to reduce the divergence angle of ki and
k f within the scattering plane to improve the energy and momentum resolution.
Inserting a collimator has been used to greatly alleviate this issue. Collimator con-
tains many parallel-arranged sheets made of neutron-absorbing materials (Fig.3.2b).
Assuming the width of the slit between two adjacent sheets is d and the length of
the collimator (along beam direction) is L, the horizontal divergence angle for this
collimator is α = d
L . Tight collimator can improve energy and momentum resolution
but decrease neutron ﬂux. The height of collimator is usually ﬁxed.
According to Eq.3.30, higher-order neutrons with λ/n (n = 2, 3 . . .) also sat-
isfy the Bragg law used by monochromator and analyzer, and will result in strong
background. Neutron ﬁlters are usually used to remove the contamination from
higher-order neutrons. For different energy ranges, various ﬁlters can be applied.
Before monochromator, sapphire can be used as a preliminary ﬁlter to remove high

40
3
Neutron Scattering
Fig. 3.2 a Schematic of neutron triple-axis spectrometer and its components. b The layout of a
triple-axis spectrometer (IN14 in Institute Laue-Langevin). c Principles of triple-axis spectrometer.
d Momentum scan and energy scan in energy transfer-momentum (ET -Q) space. e ET -intensity
plot corresponding to the energy scan in (d)
energy neutrons (>100meV). PG ﬁlter is usually used between monochromator and
sample to remove the λ/2 neutrons and reduce the background. PG ﬁlter has high
transmission efﬁciency for speciﬁc wavelengths and can scatter the correspond-
ing λ/2 neutrons. For instance, the transmission efﬁciency for the neutrons with
λ ≈2.36 Å(ki ≈2.662Å−1, Ei = 14.7meV) is 80%, while the corresponding λ/2

3.3 Neutron Scattering Spectrometry
41
and λ/3 neutrons are blocked by 99% and 98%, respectively. Similar energy win-
dows for PG include 13.7, 30.5 and 41meV. Another two important ﬁlters for lower
energies are Be and BeO polycrystals, which are usually used in cold neutron triple-
axis spectrometer. For instance, the Be ﬁlter cooled by liquiﬁed N2 can effectively
remove the neutrons above the energy of 5meV.
On the ﬂight path of neutron, some diaphragms can be placed before and after the
sample, the sizes of the diaphragms can be adjusted to reduce the background. The
scattered neutrons will be analyzed and then arrive at the detectors (Fig.3.2a). The
mostly used neutron detector is 3He tube. The nuclear interaction between neutron
and 3He (n + 3He →p +3H +764keV) generates charged particles which can be
detected.
3.3.1.2
Neutron Scattering Measurement
Figure3.2 shows the “W” geometry of the triple-axis spectrometer, which can be
different for different spectrometers. Looking towards the direction neutron travels
along, if the instrument components after next axis is on the right, we record it as “+”,
otherwise “−”. The “W” geometry shown in Fig.3.2a, b is “+ −+” conﬁguration.
In neutron scattering measurement, exit energy E f (k f ) is usually ﬁxed. Energy
transfer ET = Ei −E f is determined by incident energy Ei (ki) controlled by the
monochromator. Momentum transfer depends on the scattering angle 2θ and the
sample rotating angle ψ (relative to ki) shown in Fig.3.2c.
The procedures for a neutron scattering experiment using TAS is as follows.
The ﬁrst step is to align sample and determine the scattering plane by ﬁnding
two nuclear Bragg peaks and input their corresponding instrumental parameters
(2θM, ψ, 2θ, 2θA) into the controlling software, where 2θM(2θA) is the scattering
angle for monochromator (analyzer). The two reciprocal lattice points within the scat-
tering plane determine a UB matrix, by which the parameters (2θM, ψ, 2θ, 2θA) for
any point in this scattering plane can be calculated. For example, the [H, 0, L] scatter-
ing plane of BaFe2As2 can be determined by ﬁnding and optimizing the nuclear peaks
(200) and (006), for which the 2θ are known from the lattice parameters of BaFe2As2.
Having determined the UB matrix, every point in the (Q, ET ) space covered by the
TAS can be automatically calculated and reached. According to the scan input into the
controlling software, the system will drive the six motors (θM, 2θM, ψ, 2θ, θA, 2θA)
to the corresponding parameters. Note there are two motors for monochromator and
analyzer. For monochromator, 2θM is the motor for rotating the monochromator,
whereas θM is the motor controlling the opening angle of the shielding for the mono-
chromator. It is also this case for the analyzer. At some spectrometers, these six
angles are named as A1–A6. These six motors can drive the spectrometer to speciﬁc
positions in (Q, ET ) space.
Figure3.2c is the operating principles of TAS. Since E f is usually ﬁxed, the
energy transfer can only be tuned by changing Ei. In Fig.3.2c, the parameters for
a point in (Q, ω) space, ki, 2θ and ψ are known, and the energy transfer is ET =
ℏω = Ei −E f . We then have

42
3
Neutron Scattering
Q2 = k2
i + k2
f −2kik f cosφ,
(3.33)
ℏ2Q2
2mn
= 2Ei −ℏω −2

Ei(Ei −ℏω)cosφ,
(3.34)
which determines the kinetic limit of TAS, that is, the area in (Q, ω) space that
can be reached. In other words, whether a (Q, ω) point can be reached depends on
whether (ki, kf, Q) can form a closed triangle. Since TAS measure point by point
in (ki, kf, Q) space, Q and ω are decoupled to some extent. Two types of scans are
usually used, constant energy scan (momentum scan) and constant momentum scan
(energy scan), which are shown in Fig.3.2d, e. Another advantage of TAS is that it
can be used for polarized neutron scattering experiment, see Ref. [3, 11] for details.
3.3.1.3
Resolution of TAS
Because of the divergence angle of neutron beam, and the mosaics of monochromator
and analyzer, the energy and momentum resolution of TAS has its proﬁle in (Q, ω)
space. The resolution function can be analyzed through its projection onto the planes
determined by any two of the four axes (ω, Q∥, Q⊥, Qz) of the (Q, ω) space [10]. Q∥
represents the direction parallel to Q, and Q⊥the direction perpendicular to Q, in the
scattering plane. Qz denotes the direction normal to the scattering plane. Since all the
measurements are performed in the scattering plane, the height of the collimators is
usually set to be large to integrate the signals along vertical direction. This results in a
coarse Qz, which is not crucial. Therefore, we do not need to consider the dimension
Qz. The resolution function form an ellipsoid in the space of (ω, Q∥, Q⊥). We need
to consider only the projection of this ellipsoid onto any planes in the (ω, Q∥, Q⊥)
space (Fig.3.2d). The resolution ellipsoid is determined by four collimator (neutron
source to monochromator, monochromator to sample, sample to analyzer, analyzer
to detector), three mosaics (sample, monochromator, analyzer), two wave vectors
(ki, k f ) and one spectrometer conﬁguration [12].
3.3.2
Time-of-Flight Spectrometry
Neutron time-of-ﬂight spectrometry is a neutron scattering technique characterizing
the energy of neutron by its time of ﬂight from neutron source to detector, and the
momentum by its energy and scattering angle. In this section, we will introduce the
principles and the instrumentation for time-of-ﬂight spectrometry based on spallation
neutron source.

3.3 Neutron Scattering Spectrometry
43
3.3.2.1
Spallation Neutron Source
Different from nuclear reactor neutron source where neutrons are generated by
self-sustained nuclear chain reaction, spallation neutron source produces neutrons
through bombarding neutron-rich heavy elements (such as tungsten, mercury) with
high energy protons accelerated by synchrotron [2].
Taking ISIS spallation neutron source as an example, we describe the procedures
for producing neutrons below. ISIS spallation source contains an 800MeV proton
synchrotron which can generate 2.5 × 1013 protons in every pulse, with an average
ﬂux of 200 µA. ISIS consists of several components (Fig.3.3) [2]. (1) A linear accel-
erator used to accelerate the H−ions injected into the accelerator to 70MeV. Here,
the injected H−pulses have a frequency of 50Hz and a width (in time domain) of
500µs. The average ﬂux is 20mA. Afterwards, the electrons of these H−ions will
be removed at (2) an injection module at which the H+ ions (protons) will be injected
into the synchrotron. The injected proton pulse will be accelerated to 800MeV within
10ms and split into two narrower pulses (90ns in width) with a time difference of
210ns between them. Here the average proton ﬂux reaches 200 µA. After the accel-
eration in the synchrotron, the proton pulses will be guided out at (3). The extracted
proton pulses will be guided by quadropolar magnet through the (4) extracted proton
beam and arrive the (5) target station, where high energy proton pulses strike the
heavy element target and generate high energy neutrons. The power of the spallation
process is 160kW.
MAPS
MERLIN
ISIS Spallation Neutron Source
(1)
(2)
(4)
(3)
(5)
Fig. 3.3 Schematic of the ISIS spallation neutron source. The two time-of-ﬂight neutron scattering

3.3.2.2
Time-of-Flight Spectrometer
In Fig.3.4, we take MERLIN spectrometer in ISIS spallation neutron source to show
the principles of the direct geometry time-of-ﬂight (TOF) spectrometer. Time of
ﬂight of neutron from the source/moderator to the position-sensitive detector (PSD)
is the essential part of the TOF spectrometry, by which magnetic excitations in large
area of the (Q, ω) space can be measured simultaneously.
Figure3.4a shows the components ranging from target to detector. The neutrons
generated at target have very high energy and will be under moderated at the mod-
erator, after which the neutrons mostly fall into the energy range between ∼10 and
∼1000 meV. With high energy (above 100meV) neutrons, TOF spectrometer at
spallation neutron source is ideal for probing high energy magnetic excitations.
Afterwards, the neutron pulse enters the beamline for TOF spectrometer. To record
the time of ﬂight of the neutrons, we can set the moment at which the neutrons
depart from the moderator as t = 0 (Fig.3.4). Then the neutrons arrives at the sloppy
chopper which remove the very high energy neutrons. Note that since the neutron
pulse frequency of ISIS is 50Hz, the sloppy chopper must have the same frequency
and a proper phase.
Subsequently, the neutron pulse reaches the the Fermi chopper (also the mono-
chromator) which consists of a group of arc slits made by neutron-absorbing mater-
ial. Distinct from the monochromator in TAS, in which monochromatic neutrons are
selected via Bragg reﬂection, the rotating Fermi chopper “chop” speciﬁc neutrons
according to their moment (tc) arriving at the Fermi chopper. When tc match the
moment (t f ) that the opening of the slits arrive at the neutron ﬂight path, this part of
neutrons can go through, otherwise they will be absorbed or scattered. t f is called
chopper delay. Adjusting the chopper delay to match different tc, monochromatic
neutrons with different wavelengths can be selected [2].
Chopper delay is an essential parameter for selecting monochromatic neutrons.
As depicted in Fig.3.4a, b, the distance between the moderator and the Fermi chopper
is L0, then the relation between t f and neutron energy can be expressed as:
Ei = 1
2mn
 L0
t f
	2.
(3.35)
Therefore, t f determines the incident neutron energy.1 For ﬁxed t f (Ei), the chopper
frequency ν controls the time window (t f ) of the slit opening to neutrons. Higher ν
selects more monochromatic neutrons and lead to better energy resolution, but lower
neutron ﬂux. Since the sloppy chopper rotates with a frequency s = 50Hz (also the
neutron pulse frequency), the frequency ν of the Fermi chopper must be integer times
of the sloppy chopper
ν = ns
(3.36)

Thanks to the fast development of the large-area PSD, the spectrometer can record
the ttot and the scattering angle φ for every scattered neutron. The relation between

3.3 Neutron Scattering Spectrometry
47
Q and ℏω for every scattered neutron can be calculated from the parameters ki, ttot
and φ.
The detector bank of the MERLIN spectrometer is composed of 300 tubes (3m
long and 25mm in diameter) ﬁlled by several bars of 3He gas. As shown in Fig.3.4c,
the detector bank surrounds the sample position along both vertical and horizontal
directions. Its coverage in angle along horizontal direction is −45◦to 135◦and
vertical from −30◦to 30◦, with a coverage of 3.1 solid angle. The spatial resolution
of the 3He tube is the diameter along horizontal direction and 20 mm along vertical
direction. The whole detector bank has 50000 pixels and can cover several Brillouin
zones at a moderate Ei (such as 200meV) for solids (such as cuprates and iron
pnictides). Combined with electronic parts and controlling software, PSD is the
essential part of a TOF spectrometer [2].
3.3.2.3
Characteristics of TOF Spectrometry
Equations3.43 and 3.44 are the kinetic limits of neutron scattering measurement and
have been derived in describing the principles of TAS (Eqs.3.33 and 3.34). In the
measurements on TAS, the exit energy E f is usually ﬁxed and the incident energy
Ei (ki) will be systematically changed to perform a scan in the (Q, ω) space. The
momentum Q and the energy transfer ℏω can be controlled independently. The count-
ing time for every (Q, ω) point is in the order of minute. However, TOF measurement
need to be performed with a ﬁxed ki and sample angle ψ for much longer count-
ing time (several hours or even longer), in which the scattered neutrons follow the
kinetics determined by Eqs.3.43 and 3.44 and fall onto a paraboloid in (Q∥, Q⊥, ω)
space (Fig.3.5a). Here, Q∥is the direction parallel to ki and Q⊥perpendicular to ki.
This means that Q and ℏω are coupled through Eqs.3.43 and 3.44, which must be
taken into account in TOF measurements.
The dynamic structure factor S(Q, ω) is a function in the (Q, ω) space. The
TOF measurements of S(Q, ω) can only be performed in three-dimensional space,
resulting in a coupling between ET and Qz which is also determined by Eq.3.44.
For example, Fig.3.5b shows the projection of the scattering paraboloid onto the
[H, 0, L] scattering plane of BaFe2As2, with ki ∥L. As we can see, the ﬁx ki can
cover several Brillouin zones along L such as L = 1, 3, 5 . . .. The blue curves are
constant-energy curves. The energy transfers in these identical Brillouin zones are
different, leading to an advantage that one can get several energy transfers corre-
sponding to the same reduced in-plane wave vector. As shown in Fig.3.5b, it is
apparent that the identical in-plane wave vector (1, 0) at L = 1, 3 and 5 correspond
to different energy transfers. This advantage greatly improves the measuring efﬁ-
ciency. On the other hand, for measuring the signal at certain ET and a speciﬁc Q,
one must calculate the correct ki using Eq.3.44.
Havingdiscussedthescatteringparaboloidforﬁxed(ki, ψ),weneedtothinkabout
the elementary excitations in the systems studied by TOF measurements. Layered
compounds such as cuprates and iron pnictides exhibit very weak c-axis magnetic
coupling, leading to weak L dependence of their magnetic excitations. In this case,
