The overlap between the neutron scattering paraboloid and the S(Q, ω) dispersion
is the signal that can be probed by TOF spectrometry. Figure3.5c is a schematic
for the overlap between the paraboloid and the dispersion in ℏω-[1 0 −1] plane.
The corresponding intensity along energy shows the signal at the overlapping points
clearly. Figure3.5d is an example in a real material KCuF3 [18]. The dispersion
of the S(Q, ω) and the scattering paraboloid are projected to the ET -L plane. The
red curves denote the scattering paraboloids with two different incident energies
Ei = 150 and 100meV. The overlapping parts are marked by thick brown curves
S1 to S3. Correspondingly, the real signal that can be probed by TOF (S1–S4) are
shown in Fig.3.5e. The decrease of the signal with increasing momentum is mainly

3.3 Neutron Scattering Spectrometry
49
caused by the magnetic form factor of Cu2+. Figure3.5f shows the magnon dispersion
for La1−xCaxMnO3 and a scattering paraboloid cutting the dispersions. The probed
signal will be two circles in [Qx, Qy, ET ] space, as indicated by the red circles in
Fig.3.5f.
Sometimes, in order to decouple the energy transfer ET from the momentum Qz
and obtain full dispersion of an elementary excitation in a three dimensional material,
the TOF measurements need to be performed with various (ki, ψ) and the data need
to be combined to a 4D dataset by special data analyzing software (such as Horace)
[20, 21]. Fortunately, as we have mentioned, several important magnetic systmems
show low dimensional magnetic properties. For example, the cuprate and iron-based
superconductors exhibit weak magnetic coupling along c axis and weak dispersion
along L. Therefore, the full dispersion for the magnetic excitations in these systems
can be performed with ki∥L. It is also this case for other low dimensional systems.
The collected data can be calibrated and converted to absolute value by their
comparison with the phonon spectrum of the same sample or the incoherent scattering
of a standard vanadium sample [12, 22]. Vanadium can be used as a standard because
its cross section is well known and dominated by isotropic incoherent scattering. Its
incoherent scattering cross section (500 barns) is 250 times of its coherent cross
section (2 barns).

Chapter 4
Phase Diagram and Avoided Quantum
Criticality in BaFe2−xNixAs2
In this chapter, we describe the determination of the magnetic and structural phase
diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2 using high resolution synchrotron x-ray and neutron
diffractions. The detailed doping evolution of the orders close to optimal doping
reveals that the purported quantum criticality in electron-doped BaFe2As2 is avoided
by the emergence of an incommensurate antiferromagnetic order. We also ﬁnd a
strong magnetoelastic coupling in the BaFe2−xNixAs2 system.
4.1
Background and Motivation
A determination of the structural and magnetic phase diagram in correlated materials
is essential for understanding their ground states and dynamic properties. In the iron
pnictides, superconductivity emerges at the border of both structural and antiferro-
magnetic (AF) orders (Fig.4.1). This motivates the exploration of quantum critical
points (QCP), where the transition temperatures for such orders are continuously sup-
pressed to zero by a nonthermal control parameter. Figure4.1 brieﬂy introduces the
concept of QCP and the possible quantum criticality in carrier-doped BaFe2As2. A
quantum phase transition (QPT) is driven by quantum ﬂuctuations instead of thermal
ﬂuctuations that drive classical phase transitions. Figure4.1a illustrates the general
concept of a QPT [1]. Consider a system described by Hamiltonian H(g) = H0+gH1,
in which H0 commutes with H1 and g is a nonthermal control parameter such as
magnetic ﬁeld, pressure, or doping. With increasing g, the ground state and the ﬁrst
excited state of this system could be reversed at g = gc (Fig.4.1a), after which the
ﬁrst excited state becomes the new ground state. This abrupt change of the ground
state at gc is a QPT, the point g = gc, T = 0 is the QCP. A second-order transition
at T > 0 could be connected to a QCP. As shown in Fig.4.1b, the second-order
phase transition temperature is gradually suppressed with increasing g, as well as the
phase ﬂuctuating region. The transition temperature is completely suppressed and

tunable dimensionless coupling. If H0 and H1 commute and independent of g, level crossing between
groundstate and the ﬁrst excited state could occur. This crossing corresponds to a quantum phase
transition [1]. b A possible phase diagram for a system bearing quantum critical point. A classical
second order phase transition in T > 0 region terminates at the quantum phase transition marked by
gc. The shaded area is the phase-ﬂuctuating region for classical second order phase transition. [1]
c Phase diagram of BaFe2(As1−xPx)2. The red areas mark the non-Fermi liquid region determined
by resistivity measurements, which suggests a quantum critical point beneath the superconducting
dome [2, 3]. Reprinted with permission from [2]. Copyright 2010 by the American Physical Society.
d Phase diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2 determined by transport and magnetic measurements [4, 5].
Reprinted with permission from [5]. Copyright 2010 by the American Physical Society
the phase ﬂuctuations converge to g = gc and T = 0, which is the QCP [1], as we
have mentioned above.
In doped BaFe2As2 systems, the parent compound exhibits simultaneous struc-
tural and magnetic phase transitions at Ts ≈TN ≈138 K. Upon doping, both transi-
tions are smoothly suppressed and disappear at optimal doping, suggesting possible
structural and magnetic QCPs in doped BaFe2As2 systems. Speciﬁcally, possible
QCP in doped BaFe2As2 could exist at the border separating the AF order and the
paramagnetic state at T = 0 beneath the superconducting dome. Since the QCP in
iron pnictides is expected to appear near optimal doping, it could be intimately con-
nected with superconductivity and thereby has attracted substantial research interest.
Thoughquantumcriticalpointisatzerotemperatureandcannotbeprobeddirectly,
it can induce quantum critical ﬂuctuations over a large temperature region close to
the QCP. As shown in Fig.4.1c, the non-Fermi liquid behavior near optimal doping
of BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 was suggested to be driven by the quantum critical ﬂuctuations

4.1 Background and Motivation
53
associated with a QCP at optimal doping, and thereby supports the existence of a
QCP in BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 [2], which is also supported by the observation of divergent
penetration depth close to optimal doping [3].
Although quantum criticality in BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 has received much interest,
whether there is a QCP in electron-doped BaFe2As2 such as Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 and
BaFe2−xNixAs2 remain elusive and rarely studied [5, 6]. Upon electron doping of
BaFe2As2 via partially replacing Fe by Co or Ni, various experiments reveal that the
structural and magnetic phase transition temperatures Ts and TN in BaFe2−xTxAs2
(T =Co, Ni) gradually decrease and separate with increasing x and have Ts > TN
for all doping levels. In the initial x-ray and neutron scattering work on Ba(Fe1−x-
Cox)2As2, it was suggested that the separated Ts and TN smoothly extend into the
superconducting dome, resulting in distinct structural and magnetic QCPs at different
x. Subsequent x-ray [6] and neutron [7–9] scattering experiments on superconducting
BaFe2−xTxAs2 samples with coexisting AF order revealed that superconductivity
actuallycompeteswithstaticAForderandlatticeorthorhombicity.Asaconsequence,
the smoothly decreasing Ts and TN are reported to bend back below Tc, and the
orthorhombic structure above Tc for an optimally doped sample evolves back to a
tetragonal structure well below Tc (termed the “reentrant” tetragonal phase) [6].
Although x-ray and neutron diffractions have established the detailed structural
and magnetic phase diagram of Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 (Fig.2.6), similar measurements
have not been carried out on BaFe2−xNixAs2. Previous transport and magnetic mea-
surements did not provide enough details close to optimal doping (Fig.4.1d) [5].
From the determination of the detailed phase diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2, we aim
to sort out whether quantum criticality could exist in electron-doped systems. The
detailed phase diagram will also shed light on understanding the interplay between
the orders.
4.2
Sample Preparation
The ﬁrst step for determining the phase diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2 is to prepare high
quality single crystals for systematic dopings x covering the underdoped regime and
the whole superconducting dome. We have successfully grown a series of large Ba-
Fe2−xNixAs2 single crystals using self-ﬂux method [4]. The Fe2−xNixAs2 precursor
(also ﬂux) is ﬁrstly prepared by heating the mixture of pure Fe, Ni and As powder
to about 500 ◦C, staying for 20h and subsequently heating to 700 ◦C and staying for
15h. In order to make the precursor homogeneous, this procedure is usually repeated
3 times, with the ﬁnal temperature increasing by 20 ◦C in every sintering. For single
crystal growth, Ba pieces and Fe2−xNixAs2 ﬂux are loaded into a Al2O3 crucible with
a ratio Ba:Fe2−xNixAs2 = 1 : 5. The materials are sealed into a quartz tube under
high vacuum and put into a furnace. For sample growth, the materials are heated to
1180 ◦C and kept there for 10h. Afterwards, the melted materials cool down at a rate
of 5 ◦C per hour to 1050 ◦C and are then quenched to room temperature. After the
procedures is ﬁnished, single crystals can be cleaved out from the products.
Figure4.2a shows the large crystals grown by the self-ﬂux method. The average
size exceeds 1cm for all the dopings ranging from x = 0 to x = 0.3, with average

4.3
Structural and Magnetic Phase Transitions
and Magnetoelastic Coupling
In this chapter, we describe neutron and x-ray diffraction studies of structural and
magnetic phase transitions in BaFe2−xNixAs2, focusing on the dopings near optimal
superconductivity. Upon electron doping, besides the monotonic decrease of the AF
transition temperature TN, previous neutron scattering experiments on BaFe2−xTxAs2
also revealed a commensurate-to-incommensurate (IC) AF phase transition near

order coexists and competes with the superconductivity. Its origin, though suggested
to arise from Fermi surface nesting [10], is still controversial.
Although the magnetic transitions in BaFe2−xNixAs2 have received much atten-
tion, how the AF order evolves to IC order and disappear is still unclear, and the

4.3 Structural and Magnetic Phase Transitions and Magnetoelastic Coupling
57
temperature and doping evolution of the orthorhombic lattice distortion for samples
with IC AF order remains unknown.
To resolve the unsettled issues above, we have carried out neutron scattering
experiments on BaFe2−xNixAs2 with x = 0.085, 0.092, 0.096, 0.1, 0.104 and 0.108
using the RITA-II cold neutron triple-axis spectrometer at the Paul Scherrer Institute;
the HB-1A thermal triple-axis spectrometer at the High-Flux Isotope Reactor, Oak
Ridge National Laboratory; and the C5 triple-axis spectrometer at the Canadian Neu-
tron Beam Centre, Chalk River Laboratories. We have also performed high-resolution
synchrotron x-ray diffraction experiments on identical BaFe2−xNixAs2 samples using
beam line X22C at the National Synchrotron Light Source, Brookhaven National
Laboratory. RITA-II uses a pyrolytic graphite (PG) ﬁlter before the sample and a
cold Be ﬁlter after the sample with the ﬁnal neutron energy ﬁxed at Ef = 4.6
meV [11]. HB-1A spectrometer operates with a ﬁxed incident neutron energy of
Ei = 14.64meV using a double PG monochromator. The second-order contamina-
tion in the beam was removed by placing two PG ﬁlters located before and after
the second monochromator. A collimation of 48′-48′-sample-40′-68′ from reactor
to detector was used throughout the measurements. C5 uses PG as monochromator
and analyzer with ﬁxed Ef = 14.56meV. The sample orientation and setup are sim-
ilar to those described previously [11]. For the x-ray diffraction measurements, the
monochromator was Si(111) and incident beam energy was set at Ei = 10keV with
a spot size of 1 × 1mm2 on the samples. In all cases, the data were collected on
warming from base temperature to a temperature well above Ts. Although neutron
scattering probes the bulk sample, whereas the length scale for x-ray diffraction is
typically about 5micron, both techniques are measuring the intrinsic properties of
these materials.
We begin by describing our determination of the evolution from long-range AF
order to short-range IC AF order for BaFe2−xNixAs2 using neutron diffraction.
Figure4.5a–c show transverse scans along the [1, K, 3] direction at different tem-
peratures for x = 0.085 and 0.092. Consistent with earlier results [11], a well-
deﬁned commensurate AF order appears below 44K for x = 0.085. Furthermore,
the transverse scan for x = 0.85 broadens at T = 18K, resulting in a preliminary
IC AF component mixed with the main commensurate peak, as revealed by the dif-
ference between the transverse scans collected at T = 18 and 28K. The IC AF
order is well developed in x = 0.092. Therefore, x = 0.085 is the beginning for
the transition from commensurate long-range AF order to IC short-range AF order.
Figure4.5d–f shows temperature dependence of the magnetic order parameter for
x = 0.085, 0.092, 0.096, 0.1, 0.104 and 0.108. For x = 0.085, the AF order appears
approximately below TN = 44±5K and is suppressed from the onset of Tc, in agree-
ment with previous results [7, 11, 12]. Figure4.5e plots similar data for x = 0.092
and 0.096, showing TN = 40±5 and 32±5K, respectively [11]. In the previous work
on optimally electron-doped BaFe1.9Ni0.1As2 [14], it was suggested, based on cold
neutron data on mosaic crystals (0.6 g) counting 1min/point, that there is no mea-
surable static AF order. Our new measurements on the x = 0.1 sample (0.34 g) with
much longer counting time (30min/point on HB-1A) reveal a weak static AF order
with magnetic scattering 5 times smaller than that of x = 0.096 (Fig.4.5e, f). Similar

measurements on x = 0.104 also show the presence of a weak static AF order, which
is 50% smaller than that of the x = 0.1 sample. In spite of their small moments, the
temperature dependence of the magnetic order parameters for both samples indicates
that their Néel temperatures are essentially unchanged at TN = 30 ± 5K (Fig.4.5f).
Finally, we ﬁnd no evidence of static AF order for a x = 0.108 sample (0.5g) by
counting 40min/point on C5 (Fig.4.5f), indicating a gradual disappearance of the IC
AF order at x ≈0.108.
In order to directly compare the structural transition with the AF transition, the
measurements of the lattice orthorhombicity using high-resolution x-ray diffraction
have been performed on the same samples that were used in neutron scattering
measurements. Across the tetragonal-to-orthorhombic structural transition Ts, the

4.3 Structural and Magnetic Phase Transitions and Magnetoelastic Coupling
59
tetragonal peaks, for instance (2, 2, 12), will split into two orthorhombic peaks (4,
0, 12) and (0, 4, 12). Because of twinning effects, these two orthorhombic peaks
will appear to be close to each other along the same direction, as shown in Fig.2.3b.
Since ao is slightly larger than bo, |(4, 0, 12)| is smaller than |(0, 4, 12)|, resulting
in two peaks staying very close. Measuring the positions of these two peaks along a
[H, 0, L] direction can generate the corresponding ao and bo, and the orthorhombic
lattice distortion δ = (ao −bo)/(ao + bo), which is also the order parameter of the
orthorhombic phase.
In our x-ray measurements, we performed longitudinal scans along the orthorhom-
bic [H, 0, 12] direction. Figure4.6a shows the temperature-dependent scans for the
x = 0.085 sample with Tc = 16.5K. At a temperature above Ts (T = 58K), a single
resolution-limited peak is observed, in agreement with the tetragonal structure. The
single peak splits into two peaks with increasing peak separations as temperature
decreases down to Tc, as shown in the scans measured at T = 45, 30, and 17K.
Upon further cooling, the peak splitting becomes smaller below Tc, suggesting that
the sample turns back toward the tetragonal structure [6]. Figure4.6b shows temper-
ature evolution of similar scans for x = 0.096. The separated two peaks become a
single peak at T = 10.5K, in line with previous results for Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2. How-
ever, the width of the single peak is still larger than that for the peak at T = 34K,
indicating that the nearly optimal superconductor has an orthorhombic lattice dis-
tortion at T = 10.5K. In order to determine the orthorhombic lattice distortion at
even lower temperatures, we performed the same measurements using a cryostat
which can go down to 2K. The longitudinal [H, 0, 12] scans in Fig.4.6c show broad
peaks at temperatures below 10K, suggesting the presence of an orthorhombic lattice
structure even at 2K.
Fig. 4.6 Doping evolution of the orthorhombic (4, 0, 12) and (0, 4, 12) Bragg peaks of BaFe2−x-
NixAs2. The measurements in (a) (x = 0.085) and (b) (x=0.096) were performed from 10K to
high temperature (> TS). The temperature range of the measurements in (c) are from 2 to 19K.
d Measurements on the optimal doping x = 0.1. All the measurements were performed while
warming with Ei = 10keV of synchrotron x-ray. Reprinted with permission from [13]. Copyright
2013 by the American Physical Society

Phase Diagram and Avoided Quantum Criticality in BaFe2−xNixAs2
The temperature-dependent orthorhombic lattice distortion δ for x = 0.075,
0.085, 0.092, 0.096 and 0.1, extracted from the longitudinal scans shown in Fig.4.6
using δ = (ao −bo)/(ao + bo), are summarized in Fig.4.7a. Figure4.7b is a color
map of the lattice distortions plotted in Fig.4.7a. The doping and temperature depen-
dence of the lattice distortions are clearly illustrated in these two ﬁgures. The lattice
orthorhombicity emerges from Ts and increases while cooling, before a clear sup-
pression below Tc. With increasing doping, the lattice distortions decrease monotoni-
cally. In Fig.4.7a, Ts, TN and Tc are marked by vertical arrows. The lattice distortions
denoted by solid symbols are extracted from peak splittings, while the open sym-
bols obtained by assuming the peak broadenings close to Ts are induced by lattice
orthorhombicity, where peak splitting is too small to be resolved (Fig.4.6).
Figure4.8a,bcomparetheorderedmagneticmomentM2 andthelatticedistortions
for x = 0.096 and 0.1. Figure4.8c compare the doping dependence of M2 and
δ measured at 11K and 4K, respectively. The consistent trends between M2 and
δ in Fig.4.8a–c indicate a strong magnetoelastic coupling. The lattice distortion
for x = 0.1 (Tc = 20.2K) deserves special attention. In Fig.4.6d, although the
temperature dependent scans do not show peak splitting, the broadening of the single
peak persists to lowest measured temperature (T = 11 K). δ for x = 0.1 calculated
according to the peak broadening exhibits non-zero orthorhombic lattice distortions
below Ts = 30 ± 5K, and reveals a sharp cusp at Tc. We speculate that this anomaly
at Tc is induced by the response of the lattice to the superconducting ﬂuctuations
close to the optimal superconductivity, driven by strong electron-lattice coupling.
At T = 11K, the value of δ (2 × 10−5) is too small to conclusively determine
whether there is lattice distortion. However, given the M2 for x = 0.1 exhibits
similar evolution (Fig.4.5), we conclude that a weak AF order likely coexists with

With increasing doping, the monotonic decrease of the magnetic moment and lat-
tice distortions in BaFe2−xNixAs2 shown in Fig.4.8c, and the similar doping depen-
dence for Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 and BaFe2−xNixAs2 shown in Fig.4.8d suggest pos-
sible existence of QCPs for both structural and magnetic phase transitions. Solid
evidence for a magnetic QCP is still lacking, whereas ultrasonic measurements of
shear modulus imply that a structural QCP appears close to the optimal doping
[15, 16].
We also ﬁnd that the difference between Ts and TN decrease with increase doping
for x > 0.065, that is, Ts and TN tend to merge near optimal doping (Fig.4.8d). The
interpretation of this unusual doping evolution of the separation between Ts and TN
will be discussed in next section.

4.4
Avoided Quantum Criticality
The resulting phase diagram from our detailed x-ray and neutron diffraction mea-
surements are plotted in Fig.4.9a. The inset clearly illustrate the IC-AF region where
Ts and TN reconverges. Concerning the quantum criticality in BaFe2−xNixAs2, we
start our theoretical analysis from building a Landau-Ginzberg interaction:
S = SM[M] + Slat[φ] + Slat−M[M, φ],
(4.1)
where the three terms on the right side describe magnetic order, lattice and magne-
toelastic coupling. The magnetic interaction SM (the magnetization for MA and MB
sublattice) can be expressed as:

mined from our neutron and x-ray diffraction results. The PM Tet, PM Ort, AF Ort, and IC Ort
are paramagnetic tetragonal, paramagnetic orthorhombic, commensurate AF orthorhombic, and
incommensurate AF orthorhombic phases, respectively. The AF Ort, IC Ort, and PM Tet structures
in the superconducting (SC) phase are clearly marked. The inset shows the expanded view of Ts,

δ = (ao −bo)/(ao + bo). b Schematic theoretical phase diagram for an avoided quantum bicritical

in which S2 is the contributions from second order terms, including the Landau
damping γ [17, 18]. The quartic term S4 describes mode-mode interaction between
magnetic ﬂuctuations on the same and on different magnetic sublattices; the last term
has a negative coefﬁcient (−v < 0), favoring the collinear alignment of spins on the
two sublattices [19]. The lattice parts can be written as:

Phase Diagram and Avoided Quantum Criticality in BaFe2−xNixAs2

The last term describes magneto-elastic coupling, leading to an Ising magnetic order
when the lattice orthorhombicity δ ≡⟨φ⟩develops.
This model is similar to the O(3)×Z2 model reported in Ref. [20], except that
here the lattice quantumeld φ bears its own dynamics and undergoes Landau damping
s, making it inherently quantum critical with the dynamic exponent z = 3. In two
spatial dimensions, d + z=5 for the φ ﬁeld and d + z=4 for the M ﬁelds. Because
they are above/at the upper critical dimension, a quantum bicritical point for both
orders is expected in the presence of the magneto-elastic coupling η. This is similar
to the result of the O(3)×Z2 model [20], and is indicated schematically in Fig.4.9b.
Indeed, as noted above, our measurements ﬁnd that TN and Ts get closer to each other
as the QCP is approached (see Figs.4.8e and 4.9a) and the two order parameters
disappear at the same point (Fig.4.8c). However, the appearance of an emergent IC
magnetism at x ≈0.088 severely reduces the scattering rate γ and s (in addition
to modifying other parameters of the effective theory), thereby eliminating the QCP.
A QCP preempted by an emergent order is often referred to as “avoided” quantum
criticality [21–23].
In summary, the magnetoelastic coupling and the overall phase diagram implicate
a quantum bicritical point at T = 0 close to optimal doping, which is preempted by
a secondary short-range IC AF order with a very small ordered moment [11]. Our
results are important to clarifying the nature of the purported quantum critical point
in the carrier-doped iron pnictides, as inferred from NMR [24], thermoelectric [25],
and ultrasonic [15] measurements, as well as its connection with the QCP in the
P-doped iron pnictides that was predicted by theory [20] and observed by extensive
experiments [2, 26].

Chapter 5
Short-Range Cluster Spin Glass Near
Optimal Superconductivity
in BaFe2−xNixAs2
5.1
Incommensurate Magnetic Order in BaFe2−xTxAs2
(T = Co, Ni)
In Chap.4, we noted that the emergent incommensurate (IC) short-range antiferro-
magnetic (AF) order near optimal superconductivity of Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 heavily
modiﬁes the electronic properties of this doping region and preempts the purported
quantum critical point (QCP). Therefore, it is of particular interest to investigate
the nature of the IC AF order. Figure4.5 reveals that the IC AF order appears from
x = 0.085 and is well developed in x = 0.092. The commensurate and IC AF order
coexist in a region between x = 0.085 and 0.092, where the commensurate order
is gradually suppressed while the IC AF emerges, indicating they are two different
phases. Based on the spin density wave (SDW) scenario of the long-range commen-
surate AF order in iron pnictides driven by Fermi surface nesting [1], the IC AF
discovered in Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 was explained as IC SDW induced by Fermi sur-
face nesting with IC wave vectors [2]. Subsequently, neutron scattering experiment
reveals the competition between IC AF order and superconductivity in BaFe2−xNix-
As2 [3]. Although the SDW picture is qualitatively consistent with these experimental
results, the absence of the IC AF order in Cu doped BaFe2As2 challenges this sce-
nario and suggests that the impurity effects induced by doping must be taken into
account to forming a complete understanding of the nature of the IC AF order [4].
Charge carrier doping by elemental substitution is a main method to induce high
temperaturesuperconductivityinironpnictides.Whilethesubstitutionsuppressesthe
long-range AF order and enhances superconductivity gradually in the underdoped
regime by doping charge carriers, it also causes disorder and impurity scattering
enhancement since the doped atoms are distributed randomly in the sample and
serve as impurity scattering centers. This side effect caused by elemental substitution
can smear a magnetic phase transition, induce various emergent phases and destroy
possible quantum criticality [5–7]. As suggested by previous studies, the impurity
effects induced by Ni doping is stronger than that of Co doping [5, 8]. The IC AF
order does not show a well-deﬁned TN and exhibits a short correlation length, which
© Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2017
X. Lu, Phase Diagram and Magnetic Excitations of BaFe2−xNixAs2: A Neutron
Scattering Study, Springer Theses, DOI 10.1007/978-981-10-4998-9_5
67

68
5
Short-Range Cluster Spin Glass Near Optimal Superconductivity …
are common features of spin glass systems. In addition, μSR measurements reveal
inhomogeneous internal magnetic ﬁeld of the IC AF samples, which also suggests a
spin-glass nature of the IC AF order [2, 3, 9, 10]. This hypothesis is also supported by
nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) measurements on Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 samples
which speculated that cluster spin glass exists in the IC AF region, which is induced
by doping-induced disorder [11].
Although the results above suggest that the IC AF order induced by Co and Ni
dopings are likely to exhibit spin-glass behavior, the microscopic origin remains to
be explored experimentally. In this chapter, we will describe our conﬁrmation of the
cluster spin-glass state and the determination of the nature of the IC AF order in
BaFe2−xNixAs2.
5.2
Slow Dynamics of Spin Glass State
Spin glass is a spin-freezing disordered state with the spin directions frozen by the
interacting ferromagnetic and antiferromagnetic interactions randomly distributed
in the system. A spin-glass state usually bears two basic features in magnetic sus-
ceptibility, (1) the splitting (below the freezing temperature T f ) of the temperature-
dependent magnetic susceptibility measured with ﬁeld cooling (FC) and zero-ﬁeld
cooling (ZFC) and (2) the T f measured by AC magnetic susceptibility shifts up with
increasing frequency of the AC measurement [12]. In neutron scattering measure-
ment, spin-glass system usually shows short-range magnetic order and slow magnetic
dynamics. The later can be observed in quasielastic scattering channel using high
resolution neutron scattering spectrometers such as backscattering spectrometer. The
spin-freezing temperature T f can be measured by measuring the order parameter of
the short-range magnetic order. However, the slow dynamics exist over a wide range
of time scales (τ), where the energy scale of the magnetic dynamics is positively
correlated with the magnetic correlation length (ξ), and higher energy dynamics can
persist at higher temperature. This leads to an important feature of the spin-glass
system: the observed freezing temperature T f is sensitive to the energy resolution
(or frequency) of the instrument used, since better energy resolution captures slower
dynamics which exist only at lower temperature. The instrumental-resolution depen-
dence of the freezing temperature is usually used to identify the spin-glass nature of
a magnetic system [12].
In typical spin glass sytems such as Cu1−yMny alloy, Murani et al. demonstrated
that the magnetic order parameter measured by elastic neutron scattering represents
the Edwards-Anderson (ED) order parameter of a spin-glass system [13, 14]. Further-
more, it was revealed that the spin-freezing temperature T f decreases systematically
as increasing the energy resolution of the spectrometer [13, 14], similar to the fre-
quency dependence of the AC susceptibility of a spin-glass system. The ED order
parameter describes the time-evolution of the direction of a spin in a spin-glass sys-
tem. Assuming the spin direction is v1 at moment t = 0, the ED order parameter
denotes the possibility that the same spin pointing towards the same direction at

The self-correlation function I (t) exhibits exponential decay with time above T f
but show complicated structure below T f . As a comparison, the self-correlation func-

Here P(T, τα) is the probability distribution of τα at temperature T . For a magnetic

to the elastic scattering. A neutron scattering spectrometer with energy resolution
ΔE0 can resolve all the slow dynamics with time scale smaller than τ0 ∼ℏ/ΔE0.
Therefore, any dynamics with time scale longer than τ0 will be included in the elastic
scattering which is proportional to

Taken together the facts that faster magnetic dynamics (with shorter correlation
length) can persist at higher temperature, it is natural to get different spin-freezing
temperatures using different energy resolutions. A systematic study about the energy-
resolution dependent T f can determine an accurate freezing temperature and reveal
the dynamic nature of the spin-glass system.

5.3
Experimental Results and Discussions
We have measured the magnetic transition temperature (TN) of the IC AF order in the
x = 0.092 sample using neutron scattering with different energy resolutions ranging
from 1µeV to 1.5 meV. We ﬁnd TN = 37 ± 3 K in the measurements on triple-axis
spectrometer with energy resolution E = 1 meV, but only 30 ± 2 K using neutron
resonance spin-echo method with E = 1µeV. In addition, our polarized neutron
scattering experiment shows that the magnetic moment in the IC AF order is along
the ao direction, whereas no component is observed along the bo direction. Through
analyzing the possible driving force for the IC AF, we conclude that the IC AF order
is a short-range cluster spin-glass state in the context of superconductivity, coexisting
and competing with the superconductivity [9].
We performed our neutron scattering measurements on BaFe2−xNixAs2 using
SPINS, IN22, and TRISP triple-axis spectrometers at the NIST Center for Neutron
Research, Institut Laue-Langevin, and MLZ, respectively. For the measurements
on SPINS, we adopted the two-axis mode with incident energy Ei = 5 meV and
triple-axis mode with exit energy E f = 2.5 meV. In the two-axis measurements,
analyzer is absent in the exit arm of the spectrometer. In principle, any neutrons
with E f < 5 meV can be detected, resulting in an energy transfer range ET = [0, 5]
meV. In triple-axis measurements, E was set to ∼0.1meV. In the measurements
on TRISP, we adopted E f = 14.68 meV and a pyrolytic graphite ﬁlter to remove
high order (λ/2) neutrons. The instrument energy resolutions are E ≈1 meV in the
triple-axis mode and E ≈1µeV in the neutron resonance spin echo (NRSE) mode.
At last, the polarized neutron-scattering mode of the IN22 triple-axis spectrometer
was used to determine the direction of the magnetic moment for the IC AF order.
Similar to that in Chap.4, we deﬁne the wave vector Q at (qx, qy, qz) as (H, K, L) =
(qxao/2π, qybo/2π, qzc/2π) reciprocal lattice units (r.l.u) in orthorhombic notation.
In BaFe2As2, the commensurate AF order occurs at QAF = (1, 0, L) with the ordered
moment aligned along the ao axis, as shown in the inset of Fig.5.1. We focus on the
x = 0.092 and 0.096 sample of BaFe2−xNixAs2 to study the IC AF order. These
two samples host well-deﬁned IC AF order along the transverse direction at Q =
(1, ±δic, 3) (Fig.5.1).
Figure5.1a is a phase diagram of underdoped BaFe2−xNixAs2. The evolution from
commensurate AF order to the IC AF order occurs in the region of x = [0.085, 0.092]
[3]. We ﬁrst verify the SDW scenario of the IC AF order, which is driven by the
Fermi surface nesting of itinerant electrons and calculated using LDA. We adopt a
ﬁve-orbital tight-binding Hamiltonian and use random-phase approximation (RPA)
to calculate the generalized susceptibility, in which the introduction of electrons cor-
responds to Fermi-level shift of a rigid band structure [2]. Figure5.1b displays the
Fermi surface for x = 0.875, with the arrows denoting the nesting wave vector from
hole pocket to electron pocket. The resulting δic is 0.15. Figure5.1c is the real part of
the generalized susceptibility χRPA for x = 0.096, generating δic = 0.17. Figure5.1d
shows the three-dimensional Fermi surface of x = 0.0875, corresponding the nest-
ing in Fig.5.1b. In Fig.5.1e, we compare the doping evolution of the experimental

Figures5.3 and 5.4 summarize the measurements performed on the TRISP triple-
axis spectrometer. TRISPis anewspectrometer combiningtheﬂexibilityof thetriple-
axis spectrometer in covering large S(Q, ω) space and the high energy resolution
(E ≈1µeV) of the neutron-resonance spin-echo (NRSE) technique in probing
magnetic dynamics [18, 19]. NRSE is a neutron spin-echo (NSE) technique using
bootstrap resonant coils to reduce the neutron ﬂight path of spin-echo process. NSE
can measure the pair-correlation function and the self-correlation function I (t) [16].
Regular NSE spectrometer can measure the I (t) over the time scale from 10−12s to
10−8s. Theoretical analysis of the line shape of the I (t) is crucial for understanding
the slow dynamics in the studied magnetic system. In the case of the NRSE, the
module installed on the TRISP spectrometer can cover only the time range from
1 ps to 70 ps, for the sake of ﬂexibility and compactness. Through ﬁtting the I (t)
in speciﬁc material, the energy width of the elementary excitations (or quasielastic
scattering) can be determined with an energy resolution of 1µeV. The NRSE on
TRISP has been used in probing the energy width of phonon and magnon in some
materials [20, 21]. In our studies of the IC AF order, we measure the energy width
of the quasielastic scattering associated with the (1, 0, 3) magnetic Bragg peak [7].
The relaxation-time dependent polarization P(τ) of the scattered neutrons is
equivalent to the function I (Q, t). The signal directly collected by NRSE is the
neutron intensity as a function of neutron precession phase in the NRSE module, in
which the phase is varied by translating one of the resonant coil. Each measurement
like that in Fig.5.3a is carried out at speciﬁc temperature (T ) and relaxation time (t).

Detailed principles of the NRSE measurements can be found Ref. [19]. The periodic
modulation of the detected intensity can be described by

as the red dashed square in Fig.5.3b. I0 is the total magnetic scattering intensity, the
same as that measured by triple-axis mode, since the NRSE process will not reduce
the magnetic scattering signal.
The dynamic structure factor S(Q, ω) for quasielastic scattering (and diffuse scat-

In the measurements on TRISP, we ﬁrst measure the energy scans of the IC AF
order at Q = (1, 0, 3) using triple-axis mode. The results are shown in Fig.5.3a.
The energy scan measured at T = 57 K is subtracted as a background from the
scans collected at T = 4 K and 19 K. These energy scans can be well described by a
Lorentzian. The ﬁtting results is the same as the energy resolution of the spectrometer
(∼1meV), indicating that the energy scale of the slow magnetic dynamics is much
smaller than 1meV. The red line denotes the energy resolution of the NRSE mode (∼1
µeV). In the triple-axis mode measurements, any dynamics slower than ℏE ≈1
ps cannot be distinguished from the elastic scattering. As we have introduced, NRSE
can precisely determine the energy width Γ in the range from 1µeV to 100µeV
with E ≈1µeV. Figure5.4b shows the temperature-dependent Γ . It is clear that
the static order evolves into quasielastic scattering above 30 K, indicating that the
static order disappear above 30 K. Taken together the total scattering intensity I0
(integrated intensity of S(Q, ω)) and the ﬁtted , we can obtain the magnetic order
parameter with E ≈1µeV [red diamonds in Fig.5.4c]. The magnetic transition
temperature is determined as TN = 30 ± 2 K. With the triple-axis mode (E ≈1
meV), the transition temperature TN = 36 ± 3 K is much higher than that measured
using NRSE. The difference between these two transition temperature is caused by

76
5
Short-Range Cluster Spin Glass Near Optimal Superconductivity …
the presence of the slow magnetic dynamics between 1µeV and 1meV. The spins are
likely to be frozen below T f = 30 ± 2 K, where the magnetic dynamics are at least
slower than τ ∼ℏ/E ≈6.6 × 10−10s, in agreement with the magnetic dynamics
in typical spin-glass systems [22].
Another way to understand the origin of the IC AF order is to determine its
magnetic moment direction. In an ideal IC SDW state along [K direction, there
should be either ordered moment or moment modulations along the IC AF ordering
or the bo-axis direction [23]. In order to verify this idea, we carried out polarized
neutron diffraction to determine the ordered moment direction in the x = 0.096
sample on the IN22 triple-axis spectrometer [24]. For this experiment, the x = 0.096
sample was aligned in the [H, 0, 3H] × [0, K, 0] scattering plane. As shown in
Fig.5.4a, the incident neutron beam is polarized along [H, 0, 3H](x), [0, K, 0] and
[−3H, 0, H](z, perpendicular to x and y) directions, and the out-going neutrons
can be analyzed in the same three channels. For the spin-ﬂip cross sections, we
focus on the xx, yy and zz components σSF
x , σSF
y
and σSF
z . The magnetic moment
components My and Mz can be derived from cMy = σSF
x
−σSF
y
and cMz = σSF
x
−
σSF
z , where c = (R −1)/(R + 1) and R is the spin ﬂipping ratio [24]. As can be seen
in Fig.5.5b, the magnetic components along bo axis is Mb = My. The Mz contains the
contributions from both Ma and Mb: Mz = Masin2θ + Mccos2θ. Therefore, Mb can
be calculated from σSF
x
and σSF
y . Figure5.5c shows the rocking curves of σSF
α
(α =
x, y, z) across Q = (1, 0, 3), from which we get Mb ∼cMy and Mz ∼c(Masin2θ +
Mccos2θ). The results are shown in Fig.5.5d. There is no magnetic moment observed
along bo axis. Since the data are measured by rocking curves, we are effectively
measuring the mosaic distribution of the longitudinally ordered AF state. Figure5.5e
illustrate this hypothesis. The magnetic moments in different area slightly deviate
from the ao axis.
We consider two possibilities to understand the origin of the IC AF state, as
depicted in Fig.5.5e, f. Since the ordered moments of the IC AF phase are aligned
along the ao-axis, the observed IC peaks could be the mosaic distribution of the AF
phase in the context of the superconducting background, as shown in the green areas
of Fig.5.5e. A mosaic distribution of the magnetic moments could result in a broad
peak centered at the AF wave vector, rather than the transverse IC AF order. On the
other hand, this hypothesis cannot describe the magnitude and the doping dependence
of the incommensurability. It also does not explain the lattice orthorhombicity in
the IC AF state. Therefore, it is unlikely to be a correct description for the IC AF
phase. The second possibility is the that the IC AF order may arise from the moment
amplitude modulation along the bo axis Fig.5.5f. In this case, an incommensurability
of δ = 0.03 requires a spin-density modulation with a period of 15bo ≈80 Å, which
is larger than the observed ∼50Å correlation length.
In principle, the IC AF order favors the orthorhombic structure and cannot exist
in the tetragonal unit cell of BaFe2−xNixAs2. The orthorhombic lattice distortions
in the IC AF phase have been clearly observed in x-ray diffraction experiments [see
Chap.4]. When superconductivity emerges below Tc, the lattice orthorhombicity
and the magnetic moment of the AF order are gradually suppressed while cool-

ing. This is in line with the picture that IC AF order is intimately correlated with
the lattice orthorhombicity. If optimal superconductivity in BaFe2−xNixAs2 favors a
tetragonal structure, the IC AF state should exist only in the orthorhombic distorted
patches in the matrix of the paramagnetic tetragonal phase below TN, as illustrated
in the red area of Fig.5.5e. Since the lattice orthorhombicity should evolve gradu-
ally from the tetragonal to orthorhombic area, the IC AF order could arise from the
moment amplitude modulation along the bo axis across the crossover from tetrag-
onal to orthorhombic state. Below Tc, the volume fraction of the superconducting
tetragonal state increases with cooling at the expense of the IC AF orthorhombic
phase. This picture is consistent with a cluster spin glass (or amplitude spin glass)
and much different from a spin-density wave in nested Fermi surfaces.

Nematic Spin Correlations in Detwinned

6.1
Nematic in Iron Pnictides
A “nematic” phase was ﬁrstly proposed to describe a state of cigar-shaped molecules
in liquid crystal, in which the molecules have no positional order, but their long axes
are preferentially aligned in one direction (similar to C2 symmetry) [1]. Molecules
in nematic phase can ﬂow like a liquid. Meanwhile, they form a symmetry broken
(anisotropic) pattern like a crystal. Therefore, the nematic phase can be viewed as
an intermediate state between an isotropic liquid with O(3) symmetry and a highly
ordered crystal in view of the broken symmetry. When entering the nematic state,
the nematic transition corresponds to the breaking of the O(3) symmetry, which is
associated with the cigar shape of the molecule.
In correlated electron material, the electronic state usually has the same symmetry
as that of structure. However, the presence of the electron correlation sometimes can
organize the electrons to form certain anisotropic state which brokes the underlying
structural symmetry, which is termed “electronic nematic” [2]. Usually, electron
correlation breaks the C4 symmetry and drives the system into a C2 anisotropic
state. Electronic nematic state has been discovered in underdoped YBa2Cu3O6+x
and ultraclean Sr3Ru2O7, as indicated by their resistivity anisotropy and anisotropic
magnetic excitations [2–5].
The electronic nematic phase in doped BaFe2As2 has attracted substantial research
interests since its discovery as an electronic anisotropy revealed by resistivity mea-
surements of the uniaxial-stress detwinned single crystals of 122 systems [6, 7]. The
resistivity anisotropy, deﬁned as ψ = (ρb −ρa)/(ρb + ρa)[or ψ = ρb/ρa], appears
from a temperature (termed T ∗) well above the structural transition (Fig.6.1a) [6], as
we have discussed in Chap.2. Below T ∗, the resistivity ρb along the orthorhombic b
axis is larger and exhibits insulating behavior while the resistivity ρa shows metallic
behavior. This electronic anisotropy measured under constant uniaxial pressure per-
sists in underdoped and optimally doped regime and exhibits a maximum at certain
doping in Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2, as shown in Fig.6.1a [6]. Its temperature differential
reveals that the nematic phase transition occurs at Ts, while the anisotropy above Ts

81

could arise from nematic ﬂuctuations. Since the lattice orthorhombicity in the parent
compound BaFe2As2 is only δ = 3.6 × 10−3, and the orthorhombicity decreases
monotonically with increasing doping, the nematicity cannot be simply explained as
being driven by the orthorhombicity but should be electronic in origin. Therefore,
the electronic nematicity in iron-based superconductors is suggested to be driven by
spin ﬂuctuation, orbital ﬂuctuation or anisotropic impurity scattering.
After its discovery, the nematic phase was also found in other electronic properties
probed with different techniques such as orbital ordering, anisotropic optical con-
ductivity, anisotropic electronic state [8–20]. Figure6.1b shows the split between dyz
(along -X) and dxz (along -Y) orbitals in underdoped Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 which
exhibits similar temperature dependence to the resistivity anisotropy, indicating that
the orbital ordering could be crucial in creating the electronic nematicity.
However, in the same system, Ishida et al. found that the as-grown samples
showed larger resistivity anisotropy than the annealed samples [9], as shown in
Fig.6.1c, d. They suggested that impurity scattering below Ts in the underdoped
regime was anisotropic and resulted in resistivity anisotropy. With increasing dop-

6.1 Nematic in Iron Pnictides
83
ing, it is suggested that anisotropic scattering evolves into isotropic scattering in
overdoped regime Fig.6.1e. This picture can describe the resistivity anisotropy and
casts doubt on the intrinsic nature of the electronic anisotropy. Subsequently, Allan et
al. observed anisotropic impurity states in underdoped Ca(Fe1−xCox)2As2 [10] using
STM. As shown in the inset of Fig.6.1f, the STM current map I (q, E) measured
at E = −37 meV reveals a ’dimer’-shaped electronic impurity states. Figure6.1g
shows the quasiparticle scattering corresponding to the current map in Fig.6.1f,
which exhibits a periodic structure in momentum space. These results are consistent
with the resistivity anisotropy in iron pnictides.
Meanwhile, various experimental results such as those measured by magnetic
torque of BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 [16], shear modulus of Ba(Fe1−xCox)2As2 [20, 21] and
FeSe [22] have been interpreted in a magnetic frame [12, 16]. One important scenario
is the spin-driven Ising-nematic model proposed by Fernandes et al., [12, 13, 23]
(Fig.6.2). In this model, the true nematic transition occurs at Ts (Tnematic = Ts)

Although the absence of the AF order between TN and Ts is well known, whether

the Ising-nematic model, while it has not been tested experimentally. Therefore,
exploring the anisotropy between the magnetic excitations at (1, 0) and (0, 1) is
crucial to obtaining a complete understanding of the electronic nematic order.
As shown in the phase diagrams of doped 122 systems including the Ising-nematic
order (Fig.6.3), the nematic order emerges at Tnem = Ts and exhibits ﬂuctuations
in a wide temperature range above Ts. According to the Ising-nematic scenario,
the temperature range TN < T < Ts where nematic order locates, and the region
above Ts where nematic ﬂuctuations persist, are of particular interest to exploring the
effect of nematic order and nematic ﬂuctuations in doped 122 systems. The results
will shed light on the microscopic origin of the electronic nematic phase in iron-
based superconductors. BaFe2−xNixAs2 is an ideal system for such research purpose
because of the large separation of Ts and TN in its underdoped regime. In addition,

6.2
Experimental Methods
Mechanical devices for detwinning iron-pnictide single crystals were ﬁrstly invented
by Tanatar et al. [7] and Chu et al. [6]. Samples were detwinned by externally applied
uniaxial tensile or compressive stress in these devices. Later, Chu et al. developed the
methodology to measure the elastoresistivity under constant strain (ε) using piezo
stacks [24]. However, these devices are not proper for neutron scattering experi-
ments which requires large single crystals (>0.1g) to study magnetic excitations.
The ﬁrst neutron diffraction experiment on uniaxial-pressure detwinned Ba(Fe1−x-
Cox)2As2 was performed by Chetan et al. using a complicated device [25]. And
the experiment revealed the effects of uniaxial pressure on the magnetic and struc-
tural transition. Afterwards, we designed a more effective device and developed the
method to prepare well-cut square single crystals for inelastic neutron scattering
studies. Our device is capable of detwinning large crystals with mass ∼0.1−0.5g.
The maximum force the device can apply is about 100 N, generating about 20MPa
pressure on a crystal with a sectional area of 5mm2. The efﬁciency of this device
was demonstrated by neutron diffraction experiments on BaFe2As2 [26].
Figure6.5a is a schematic of the detwinning device. The pressure is applied along
the tetragonal [110] direction. Since ao > bo, bo will be predominantly aligned along
this direction. The device is made by aluminum alloy #6061 with very low incoherent
scattering cross section. The single crystal is inserted into the slots on the right side
of the device, with the tetragonal [110] (bo) axis aligned along the pressure-applied
direction (Fig.6.5a). The uniaxial pressure is applied by the spring which can be
continuously tuned by tightening the screw on left side of the device. Under uniaxial

temperature Ts.
Assuming the distribution probability of one kind of domain as P1 and the other
(1 −P1), we get the detwinning ratio Rd = |2P1 −1|. In order to obtain high Rd,
three steps must be carefully carried out. (1) The orientation of the crystal needs to
be precisely determined by Laue diffraction, as shown in Fig.6.5b. A minor mis-
alignment (>1◦) will cause difﬁculties in aligning the sample in neutron scattering
experiment. (2) While cutting the sample, the sectional surface must be parallel to
the c-axis, otherwise the sample can be easily broken under pressure. (3) The sec-
tional surface must be smooth to apply the uniaxial pressure homogeneously. To
satisfy these three requirements, we adopted real-time Laue camera to determine the
orientation of single crystals and cut single crystals by high-precision wire saw.
In our experiments, to probe the magnetic excitations at Q1 = (1, 0, 1) and Q2 =
(0, 1, 1) simultaneously, we aligned the sample and the device in a scattering plane
determined by vectors [112] and [1-10] (Figs.6.5c and 6.6). The green and orange
arrows in Fig.6.6 show the two momentum-scanning directions in our measurements.

6.3
Nematic Spin Correlations in the Tetragonal State
of BaFe2−XNixAs2
To reveal the effects of the nematic order and ﬂuctuations on the magnetic exci-
tations, we use neutron scattering to study the uniaxial-strained parent compound
BaFe2As2 (TN = 138 K), electron underdoped BaFe1.915Ni0.085As2 (Tc = 16.5 K,
TN = 44 K) and electron overdoped BaFe1.88Ni0.12As2 (Tc = 18.6 K) Figs.6.7, 6.8,
6.9, 6.10, 6.11 and 6.12. In the paramagnetic tetragonal state at high temperature
T > Ts > TN, the magnetic excitations at (±1, 0) and (0, ±1) should have equal
intensities, following the C4 symmetry of the tetragonal structure. This has been
conﬁrmed in previous neutron scattering reports [17]. In the AF state below TN, the
spin waves arising from the AF order should appear at AF wave vector (1, 0) and
have vanishing intensity at (0, 1). But it is still unclear whether the magnetic excita-
tions exhibit differences in the paramagnetic orthorhombic state between Ts and TN,
where the structural C4 symmetry is broken. In our studies, we ﬁnd that the magnetic
excitations indeed show intensity anisotropy between (1, 0) and (0, 1) above TN.
The emerging temperature (T ∗) of the spin excitation anisotropy [termed “nematic
spin correlation”], however, is well above Ts in underdoped regime and consistent
with that from the resistivity anisotropy [6, 9, 11]. Moreover, the nematic spin cor-
relations disappear in the overdoped x = 0.12 sample, resulting in similar doping
dependence to that for the resistivity anisotropy [6]. These similarities between the
nematic spin correlations and the resistivity indicate that they are likely intimately
connected and should share the same origin.
In the Ising-nematic model, a C4-symmetry-broken state without long range mag-
netic order is an Ising-nematic phase, which could be induced by an order by disorder
mechanism [28, 29]. However, this nematic state below Ts could also arise from the
orbital ordering induced by dxz/dyz orbital splitting [30–33]. Furthermore, the resis-

tivity anisotropy supporting the appearance of the electronic nematic, was recently
explained driven by anisotropic impurity scattering [9, 10].
In order to explore the possible spin nematic state and obtain further understanding
of the electronic nematic, we carried out inelastic neutron scattering (INS) experi-
ments using the PUMA thermal triple-axis spectrometer at the MLZ in Garching,
Germany. Horizontally and vertically curved pyrolytic graphite (PG) crystals were
used as a monochromator and analyzer. To eliminate contamination from epithermal
or higher-order neutrons, a sapphire ﬁlter was inserted before the monochromator and
two PG ﬁlters were installed before the analyzer. All measurements were done with
a ﬁxed ﬁnal wave vector, k f = 2.662 Å−1. Our annealed squared shaped single crys-
tals of BaFe2As2 (∼120mg), BaFe1.915Ni0.085As2 (∼220mg), and BaFe1.88Ni0.12As2
(∼448mg) were mounted inside aluminum based sample holders with a uniaxial
pressure of P ≈15 MPa, 7 MPa, and 7 MPa, appling along the bo axis, respectively.
In the AF state of a fully detwinned sample, the AF Bragg peaks should occur at
(±1, 0, L)(L = odd number) positions in reciprocal space. In addition, the low-
energy spin waves should only stem from the (±1, 0) positions. By contrast, in the

90
6
Nematic Spin Correlations in Detwinned BaFe2−xNixAs2
paramagnetic tetragonal phase one would expect the spin excitations at the (±1, 0)
and (0, ±1) positions to have equal intensities [17].
To see if it is indeed the case, we ﬁrst measure the magnetic order and low
energy spin waves at (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1). In addition, given that our experiments
are performed in uniaxial-strain detwinned samples, it is also important to establish
how the structural and magnetic transition temperatures are affected by the applied
pressure. Although an earlier neutron diffraction work suggested that uniaxial pres-
sure could increase the structural and magnetic transition temperatures on as-grown
BaFe2As2 [25], recent experiments on annealed samples indicated that these transi-
tion temperatures are not much affected by the uniaxial strain [26]. Figure6.7c is a
comparison of the temperature-dependent magnetic order parameters at (1, 0, 1) and
(0, 1, 1) for BaFe2As2 under zero (green symbols) and uniaxial pressure (red and
blue symbols). We ﬁnd that the BaFe2As2 sample is completely detwinned under
the applied unaxial pressure without changing TN. Similarly, the electron under-
doped BaFe1.915Ni0.085As2 is 60% detwinned and has TN ≈44 K Fig.6.7e. To test
if the tetragonal-to-orthorhombic structural phase transition in BaFe2−xNixAs2 is
affected by uniaxial stress, we plot in Fig.6.7d the temperature evolution of the
(2, −2, 0) nuclear Bragg peak of BaFe2As2. In neutron diffraction of single crystal,
strong nuclear Bragg peak is usually affected by sample mosaicity, which is called
secondary extinction effect. When a structural transition occurs, the mosaic of the
sample will also change accordingly, resulting in an abrupt change of the intensity
for some strong nuclear Bragg peaks at the transition [34–37]. The step-like feature
at Ts = 138 K results from the releasing of the neutron extinction across the struc-
tural transition. Furthermore, the intensity of the (2, −2, 0) nuclear peak deviates
clearly from the linear behavior below 150 K, indicating ﬂuctuating orthorhombic
structural domains above Ts [38]. While the uniaxial pressure has minor effects on
Ts = 138 K, it increases the ﬂuctuating orthorhombic domain temperature to about
165 K (Fig.6.7f).
Previous neutron scattering studies on BaFe2As2 revealed a 10 meV spin-wave
energy gap at (1, 0, 1)/(0, 1, 1) [39]. Figure6.8a, c and e are constant energy scans
across the (1, 0, 1) on detwinned BaFe2As2. In the AF ordered state (T = 3 K), no
magnetic excitations are observed at E = 6 meV because of the presence of the 10
meV magnon gap (Fig.6.8a). Above the magnon gap, spin waves at E = 15 meV and
19 meV are clearly observed (Fig.6.8c, e), which is consistent with previous results
[39]. Interestingly, we do not observe any spin waves with E = 6, 15 and 19 meV
at (0, 1, 1). This is in agreement with the 100% detwinning of the BaFe2As2 crystal
shown in Fig.6.7. Upon warming the sample to the paramagnetic state (T = 154 K),
the magnon gap is closed and magnetic excitations at E = 6meV can be seen at both
(1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1). However, we note that the intensity of the magnetic excitations
at (1, 0, 1) is obviously higher than that at (0, 1, 1) (Fig.6.8a, b), indicating the
existence of nematic spin correlations in the paramagnetic tetragonal state.
In order to quantitatively study the energy dependence of the spin-excitation
anisotropy (nematic spin correlations), we have carried out energy scans at (1, 0, 1),
(0, 1, 1) and their corresponding background positions at T = 154 K (Fig.6.9a,
b). The background subtracted spin excitations at (1, 0, 1) is stronger than that at

Nematic Spin Correlations in Detwinned BaFe2−xNixAs2
(0, 1, 1) in the energy range of our measurement ([4, 16] meV) (Fig.6.9c). When
warming up to T = 189 K, the same energy scans are shown in Fig.6.9b. After sub-
tracting the background, we ﬁnd that the spin excitations at (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1) have
the same intensity, indicative of the disappearance of the spin excitation anisotropy.
Figure6.9e shows the temperature dependence of the background-subtracted mag-
netic excitations at E = 6 meV. In the AF state, spin waves only appear at (1, 0, 1).
After warming up to the paramagnetic tetragonal state above Ts, the intensity differ-
ence of the magnetic excitations between (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1) vanishes at about 165
K, which is consistent with the onset temperature of resistivity anisotropy, as shown
in the inset of Fig.6.9e [40]. Since the resistivity anisotropy is accompanied by the
evolution of magnetic excitations from C4 symmetry to C2 symmetry, we conclude
that the resistivity anisotropy is an intrinsic property, rather than being induced by
anisotropic impurity scattering [9, 10].
Following the results of BaFe2As2, we have also studied the magnetic-excitation
evolution from C4 to C2 symmetry of the magnetic excitations in the under-
doped BaFe1.915Ni0.085As2 sample (Fig.6.7e). The magnetic excitations of (1, 0, 1)
and (0, 1, 1) at E = 6 meV and their temperature dependence are studied and
summarized in Fig.6.10. In previous study, neutron scattering experiment on a
x = 0.08 sample has revealed a resonance at E = 6 meV [41]. Figure6.10a, c
depict the constant-energy scans at E = 6 meV across (1, 0, 1) along two different
directions, as indicated by the blue arrows in the inset of Fig.6.10b, d. It is clear that
the magnetic excitations at (1, 0, 1) are greatly enhanced across Tc while cooling
from 48 K to 8 K. Figure6.10b, d are similar scans across (0, 1, 1), showing much
weaker magnetic excitations than that at (1, 0, 1). Figure6.10e shows the temper-
ature dependence of the magnetic excitations at (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1) with E = 6
meV. Consistent with Fig.6.10a–d, the magnetic excitations at (1, 0, 1) is stronger
that that at (0, 1, 1) above Tc. This spin-excitation anisotropy persists above Ts and
decreases gradually, without exhibiting any abrupt change at TN and Ts, as shown in
Fig.6.10e–f. Nematic spin correlations in the tetragonal state disappears at T ∗≈80
K, well above the Ts and TN, and consistent with the onset temperature of the resis-
tivity anisotropy [11].
Finally, we discuss uniaxial-strain effects on electron overdoped BaFe1.88Ni0.12
As2, which show vanishingly small resistivity anisotropy between ρa and ρb [6, 11].
Figure6.11a and show transverse momentum scans at E = 6 meV across the (1, 0, 1)
and (0, 1, 1) wave vectors below and above Tc, respectively, under the same uniaxial
pressure as that for the BaFe1.915Ni0.085As2 sample. We ﬁnd that spin excitations have
identical intensities at (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1), obeying the C4 rotational symmetry of
the underlying lattice. Figure6.11c, d show energy scans across Tc = 18.6 K at
(1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1), respectively. Spin resonance mode and spin gap form when
the sample enters the superconducting state. The temperature difference of energy
scansbetween T = 3.5Kand20 Kconﬁrmstheneutronspinresonancewithidentical
intensity at E = 7 meV for both (1, 0, 1) and (0, 1, 1) [42]. Figure6.11f plots the
temperature dependence of the scattering at E = 7 meV, which again indicates that
the applied uniaxial strain does not break the C4 rotational symmetry of the spin
excitations.

ThekeydiscoverieshavebeensummarizedinFig.6.12.Wehaveobservednematic
spin correlations in the tetragonal state of uniaxial strained BaFe2−xNixAs2. The
onset temperature (T ∗) of the spin nematic correlations is much higher than Ts
Fig.6.12 but consistent with the T ∗revealed in resistivity anisotropy. The nematic
spin correlations show similar doping dependence to the resistivity anisotropy, indi-
cating a close correlation between them.
In principle, the electronic nematic order parameter should be linearly coupled
with the lattice orthorhombicity (ϵ = ao−bo) after the C4 symmetry of the electronic

state is broken. Therefore, the nematic transition from C4 toC2 occurs simultaneously
with the tetragonal-to-orthorhombic structural transition Ts [12, 13]. Then how do we
understand the anisotropy of the low energy spin excitations between TN and T ∗? We
suggest that it can be described using the effective action for the electronic nematic
order parameter  and magnetization M1/2 of the interpenetrating Néel sublattices
[12, 13, 29]:
Seff[, M] = S0[M2

(6.1)
in which S0 is the part containing no nematic correlations (M1 · M2),1 which is
decoupled from the bosonic ﬁeld associated with the nematic correlations (q, ω),
characterized by the nematic susceptibility α(q, ω). Minimizing the action with ϵ,

Nematic Spin Correlations in Detwinned BaFe2−xNixAs2
we get ϵ = λ <  > /Cs, where Cs is shear modulus. That is, the lattice orthorhom-
bicity is linearly coupled with the nematic order parameter <  >, and both emerge
from the structural transition Ts [12]. However, the nematic ﬁeld undergoes strong
ﬂuctuations above Ts while ϵ remains zero. These ﬂuctuations could be observed in
certain dynamic channels such as the low-energy spin ﬂuctuations. Thus, we con-
clude that the scale of the onset temperature T ∗of the spin-excitation and resistivity
anisotropy represents a typical range of the nematic ﬂuctuations. T ∗is more likely
a crossover rather than a phase transition. As we have emphasized, the nematic
transition occurs at Ts.
Besides the discussions above, we have several other remarks. First, since the
uniaxial pressure used to detwin the sample can induce ﬁnite ϵ at any temperature,
the structural transition Ts will become a crossover rather than a phase transition.
However, the applied uniaxial pressure is too small to induce a detectable lattice
distortion, which is the reason that Ts remains unchanged as determined from tem-
perature dependence of the nuclear (2, 2, 0) Bragg peak Fig.6.7d, f [39]. On the
other hand, nematic ﬂuctuations could be sensitive to the shear strain since the T ∗
increases with increasing pressure, as determined from resistivity anisotropy under
different uniaxial pressures [6, 11]. Second, in the action 6.1, the variable  could
also signify the orbital order [ ∝(nxz −nyz)], which can lift the degeneracy of
the dxz and dyz orbitals of Fe. These two order parameters will be linearly coupled,
(M1 · M2) ∝(nxz −nyz), and develop non-zero expectation below Ts. Therefore,
the nematic spin correlations in BaFe2As2 are consistent with the orbital ordering
observed by ARPES [8]. Third, the nematic spin correlations persist to the under-
doped x = 0.085 sample which is close to optimal doping, where orbital ordering
was not observed by ARPES [8, 43]. Since the structural transition Ts in this sample
is suppressed to 52 K, the spin-excitation anisotropy could originate from quantum
nematic ﬂuctuations. Fourth, both the resistivity anisotropy and the nematic spin
correlations disappear in slightly overdoped samples [6, 11], indicating they are inti-
mately correlated, as shown in Figs.6.11 and 6.12. At last, the onset temperature
(T ∗) of the nematic spin correlations does not signify a thermodynamic order below
T ∗. This implies that the magnetic torque anisotropy in BaFe2(As1−xPx)2 reported
in Ref. [16] should not be in the spin channel.
To conclude, we have discovered a four-fold to two-fold transition in the low-
energy spin ﬂuctuations of uniaxial strained BaFe2−xNixAs2 at temperatures T ∗
above the structural and magnetic phase transitions. Furthermore, we note that the
temperature T ∗is also associated with the previously reported resistivity anisotropy
[6, 7, 14, 24]. Our results unveil the nematic spin correlations, establish that they
are intrinsic and intimately connected to the resistivity anisotropy, and show that
they persist in the quantum ﬂuctuation regime close to the optimally electron-doped
iron-pnictide superconductor.

Chapter 7
Magnetic Excitations in Overdoped
BaFe2−xNixAs2
Antiferromagnetic spin ﬂuctuation has been thought as an important candidate for
the “pairing glue” that mediates superconducting electron pairs in cuprates and
iron-based high-temperature superconductors [1–3]. This is suggested by the cor-
relation between spin resonance and superconductivity [4–13], and the ubiquitous
high-energy magnetic excitations throughout the whole phase diagrams [14–20].
7.1
Scientiﬁc Background
7.1.1
Spin Resonance and Superconductivity
We ﬁrst describe the correlation between spin-resonance mode and high-Tc super-
conductivity. Spin resonance is a spin-1 bosonic mode that emerges at low energy
magnetic excitation spectrum when the system enters the superconducting state.
Comparedwithnormal-statespinexcitations,thespectralweightofspinexcitationsis
redistributed below Tc, resulting in a spin resonance (peak) mode at Er(Er ≤2, 
is superconducting energy gap), and a spin gap at lower energy [4, 5, 7].
Spin resonance emerges simultaneously with superconductivity upon cooling
across Tc in unconventional superconductors, with its temperature dependence sim-
ilar to the superconducting order parameter (energy gap ) (Fig.7.1a, b). The mag-
nitude of the spin resonance is positively correlated with Tc and superﬂuid density.
For example, the spin resonance intensity in overdoped La2−xSrxCuO4 decreases
with increasing the doping and ﬁnally becomes zero in the non-superconducting
x = 0.3 sample (Fig.7.1c) [13]. Furthermore, spin resonance has been discovered
in numerous unconventional superconductors. The resonance energies in one class
of superconductors are proportional to the corresponding Tc and 2. This linear
relationship have been discussed in various reports (Fig.7.1d, e) [9, 11, 21]. These
© Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2017

Scattering Study, Springer Theses, DOI 10.1007/978-981-10-4998-9_7
101

Magnetic Excitations in Overdoped BaFe2−xNixAs2
(a)

Fig. 7.1 Spin resonance and its correlation with superconductivity in various unconventional super-
conductors. a Temperature dependence of the spin resonance in hole doped YBa2Cu3O6.93. From
[7]. b The spin resonance in iron pnictide superconductor Ba1−xK0.4Fe2As2 show similar tem-
perature dependence. Reprinted with permission from Macmillan Publishers Ltd: [12], copyright
2008. c Linear relation between the magnitude of the spin resonance and the doping levels in
La1−xSrxCuO4. Reprinted with permission from [13]. Copyright 2007 by the American Physical
Society. d Universal linear relations between resonance energy and Tc and e resonance energy and
superconducting gap in many unconventional superconductors. Reprinted with permission from
Macmillan Publishers Ltd: [11], copyright 2009
direct correlations between spin resonance and superconductivity in cuprates and
iron-based superconductors suggest that antiferromagnetic spin ﬂuctuations could
be a microscopic driving force for unconventional superconductivity. Based on this
idea, Scalapino et al. developed a formalism theory of superconductivity induced by
spin ﬂuctuations, similar to the BCS theory [2, 3]. Based on his model the magnetic
exchange energy difference between normal state and superconducting state can be
estimated from the spectral weight difference of the magnetic excitations. Several
works have demonstrated that such magnetic exchange energy difference is large
enough for superconducting condensation [22].
The microscopic origin of the spin resonance remains elusive. It was proposed to
be a transition from spin singlet to triplet of a cooper pair, without pair breaking [23,
24]. If it is indeed the case, the spin resonance was expected to split into three peaks
under magnetic ﬁeld. This proposed spin-resonance split, however, has not been
observed in neutron scattering studies under magnetic ﬁeld (up to 14 T) in cuprates
and iron-based superconductors [25, 26]. Since the resonant energy Er is smaller
than 2, the spin resonance can be treated as an exciton mode [3]. Understanding
the nature and behavior of spin resonance is still an important task in the studies of
unconventional superconductors.

7.1 Scientiﬁc Background
103
7.1.2
Ubiquitous High Energy Magnetic Excitations
Moreover, recent inelastic neutron scattering (INS) and resonant inelastic x-ray scat-
tering (RIXS) experiments reveal that high-energy magnetic excitations persist in
optimally doped and overdoped regimes in various cuprates and iron-based super-
conductors [14–17, 20, 27]. Figure7.2 shows the ubiquitous paramagnons in various
cupratesystems.Thesemagneticexcitations(paramagnons)showsimilardispersions
and intensities to that of the spin waves in their parent compounds (Fig.7.2b, c, e, g),
indicating that the exchange coupling J between the local moments remains. This is
essential for the theory of spin-ﬂuctuation induced superconductivity. Paramagnons
inoptimallydopedandoverdopedsamplestendtohavelargerenergywidth(Fig.7.2b,
e) which could be attributed to damping upon carrier doping. The paramagnons with
exchange coupling J is a matrix for the emergence of the superconductivity. Under-
standing and modeling the high energy magnetic excitations will shed light on the
relationship between antiferromagnetism and superconductivity.
7.1.3
Magnetic Excitations in Doped BaFe2As2
Magnetic excitations in iron-based superconductors and their correlation with super-
conductivity are similar to those in cuprates. However, Fermi surface nesting from

hole pockets around  point to electron pockets around X point is unique in iron
pnictides. It is suggested to enhance low-energy magnetic excitations and super-
conductivity, as we have discussed in Chap.2. Consequently, orbitals where the
Fermi surface nesting occurs determine the proﬁle of magnetic excitations [1]. As
shown in Fig.7.3, the low-energy magnetic excitations for BaFe2As2 and electron-
doped BaFe2As2 are vertically elongated ellipses (along [1, K]), while those for
hole-doped BaFe2As2 are horizontally elongated ellipses (along [H, 0]). The pro-
ﬁles of the magnetic excitations in doped BaFe2As2 systems are revealed by INS
experiments and well described by RPA calculations [17, 28]. In addition, previous
INS studies reveal that the doping dependence of the anisotropy of the low-energy
spin excitations (FWHM of the magnetic excitation along [1, K] over that along
[H, 0]) in BaFe2−xNixAs2 can also be qualitatively described by RPA calculation
based on the Fermi surface nesting scenario [29]. The consistency between exper-
imental observations and theoretical calculations suggest that the RPA calculation
captures the main features of low-energy magnetic excitations.
For BaFe2−xNixAs2, INS studies have been performed on the parent compound
and the optimally doped (x = 0.1) sample [17, 30]. These experiments reveal that
only the magnetic excitations below 100 meV are modiﬁed by electron doping. By
contrast, the magnetic excitations above 100 meV change less from x = 0 to 0.1.
The total ﬂuctuating moments for x = 0 and 0.1 are < m2 >= 3.6 ± 0.16¯2
B and

excitations from x = 0 to 0.1 has been well established, the doping evolution in the
overdoped regime remains elusive. In this chapter, we will describe our INS studies
of overdoped BaFe2−xNixAs2 with x = 0.15, 0.18 and 0.3.

7.2
Results and Discussions
Figure7.4 is a schematic phase diagram of BaFe2−xNixAs2. Solid and open red
squares markthepositions (x, T ) wherethemagneticexcitations havebeenmeasured
[17–19, 30]. Results for x = 0.15, 0.18 and 0.3 are associated with this thesis and
will be discussed in detail. We will focus on describing the electron-doping evolution
of the spin resonance, low-energy magnetic excitations, high energy excitations, and
their correlation with superconductivity.
Corresponding to the phase diagram (Fig.7.4), Fig.7.5 shows the doping and
energy dependence of the magnetic excitations in BaFe2−xNixAs2 with x = 0, 0.096
(optimal, Tc = 20 K), 0.15, 0.18 and 0.3 measured at base temperature. It is clear that
the low energy magnetic excitations evolve from long correlation length spin waves
to diffusive paramagnons and form an energy gap (∼50 meV) in x = 0.3 sample.
The spin resonance mode is observed in x = 0.096 and 0.15 but greatly suppressed
in x = 0.18. In contrast to the signiﬁcant evolution of the low energy magnetic
excitations, the high energy magnetic excitations decrease slowly with increasing
doping.
7.2.1
Spin Resonance in Overdoped BaFe2−xNixAs2
The spin resonance in BaFe2−xNixAs2 reaches its maximum in an optimally doped
x = 0.096 (Tc = 20 K) sample. With increasing doping, the spin resonance exhibit-
ing incommensurate structure along [1,K] direction is also observed in overdoped
x = 0.15 (Tc = 14 K) and 0.18(Tc = 8 K) samples. Figure7.6a, b show the spin
resonance of x = 0.15 in (E, [1, K]) plane, and in an energy cut, respectively. A
resonance at Er = 6.5 meV [Er/kBTc ≈5.4] is clearly seen. Figure7.6c, d are
momentum cuts along [1, K] and [H, 0] directions, respectively. The former shows

7.2.2
Evolution of Magnetic Excitations in Heavily
Overdoped BaFe2−xNixAs2 (x=0.3)
With further electron doping to x = 0.3 (Fig.7.4), low-energy spin excitations are
strongly suppressed and form a spin gap is formed at (1, 0) (Fig.7.5). Since low-
energy spin excitations are believed to arise from the interband scattering between
the hole pockets and the electron pockets as shown in Figs.7.3 and 7.9, low-energy
spin excitations are expected to disappear in x = 0.3 since the electron pockets
sink below the Fermi surface with increasing electron doping to x = 0.3 (Fig.7.9).
The consistent doping evolution between Fermi surface topology (Fig.7.9a–c) and
low energy spin excitations provides a solid evidence for the quasiparticle-excitation
nature of low-energy spin excitations. Since superconductivity disappears together
with low-energy magnetic excitations in x = 0.3, magnetic excitations arising from
the interband scattering should be correlated with superconductivity.

excitations above 50 meV show similar intensity and dispersion to that for x = 0
and 0.1, as shown in the momentum cuts in Fig.7.12.
From the momentum cuts in Figs.7.10, 7.11 and 7.12, we get the dispersion of
the spin excitations in x = 0.3 (Fig.7.13). Compared with the magnon dispersion
for x = 0 (solid curves in Fig.7.13), the dispersion for x = 0.3 shows similar
features, with slightly lower energy band top and an ∼50 meV spin gap. The
persistence of the magnetic excitations with well-deﬁned dispersion in x = 0.3
indicates that the magnetic excitations arise from interacting local moments, and the
magnitude of the exchange couplings is similar to that in the parent compound. As
we have discussed, the exchange coupling J is an essential interaction for the spin-
ﬂuctuation theory of superconductivity. However, as we noticed here, low-energy
magnetic excitations correlated with the interband scattering of itinerant electrons
are also necessary ingredients for creating superconductivity.
To determine the doping evolution of magnetic excitations, we have calculated
local susceptibility of x = 0.15, 0.18 and 0.3 using the method described in
Sect.7.1.4. The results are shown in Fig.7.14. Similar to the evolving trend shown in

Figs.7.5 and 7.14a shows that the low energy part of χ′′(ω) (< 80 meV) decreases
gradually with increasing doping for x > 0.096, with a spin gap (∼50 meV) opened

B/Fe(Ni), respectively. This, again, indicates that total

Since the real doping x′ is proportional to nominal doping x′ = 0.8x, the real
doping for x = 0.3 is x′ = 0.24. Assuming that the ﬂuctuating moment decreases
linearly with increasing doping, the end member BaNi2As2 will be expected to have
sizeable ﬂuctuating moment. Of course, this extrapolation is not strict since the
doping range for x′ = 0.24 is still only 0.12Ni per Fe. However, it rises a question:
whether Ni dopants have a local moment in BaFe2−xNixAs2. This question is not
easy to answer with INS, which is not element speciﬁc. The kβ emission of Fe which
can probe the magnetic moment of speciﬁc element, or RIXS which can measure
magnetic excitations arising at the resonant edge of speciﬁc element, can shed light
on this question [20, 36, 37].

m2
, we can get the size of atomic moment

m2
≈1.8μB for BaFe2As2, while the corresponding ordered moment is only
0.9μB. The difference between the atomic moment and the ordered moment indicates
that the system contains magnetism from both local moments and itinerant electrons.
